line 32: PRIDE AND PREJUDICE
line 33: 
line 34: By Jane Austen
line 35: 
line 36: 
line 37: 
line 38: Chapter 1
line 39: 
line 40: 
line 41: It is a truth universally acknowledged, that a single man in possession
line 42: of a good fortune, must be in want of a wife.
line 43: 
line 44: However little known the feelings or views of such a man may be on his
line 45: first entering a neighbourhood, this truth is so well fixed in the minds
line 46: of the surrounding families, that he is considered the rightful property
line 47: of some one or other of their daughters.
line 48: 
line 49: “My dear Mr. Bennet,” said his lady to him one day, “have you heard that
line 50: Netherfield Park is let at last?”
line 51: 
line 52: Mr. Bennet replied that he had not.
line 53: 
line 54: “But it is,” returned she; “for Mrs. Long has just been here, and she
line 55: told me all about it.”
line 56: 
line 57: Mr. Bennet made no answer.
line 58: 
line 59: “Do you not want to know who has taken it?” cried his wife impatiently.
line 60: 
line 61: “_You_ want to tell me, and I have no objection to hearing it.”
line 62: 
line 63: This was invitation enough.
line 64: 
line 65: “Why, my dear, you must know, Mrs. Long says that Netherfield is taken
line 66: by a young man of large fortune from the north of England; that he came
line 67: down on Monday in a chaise and four to see the place, and was so much
line 68: delighted with it, that he agreed with Mr. Morris immediately; that he
line 69: is to take possession before Michaelmas, and some of his servants are to
line 70: be in the house by the end of next week.”
line 71: 
line 72: “What is his name?”
line 73: 
line 74: “Bingley.”
line 75: 
line 76: “Is he married or single?”
line 77: 
line 78: “Oh! Single, my dear, to be sure! A single man of large fortune; four or
line 79: five thousand a year. What a fine thing for our girls!”
line 80: 
line 81: “How so? How can it affect them?”
line 82: 
line 83: “My dear Mr. Bennet,” replied his wife, “how can you be so tiresome! You
line 84: must know that I am thinking of his marrying one of them.”
line 85: 
line 86: “Is that his design in settling here?”
line 87: 
line 88: “Design! Nonsense, how can you talk so! But it is very likely that he
line 89: _may_ fall in love with one of them, and therefore you must visit him as
line 90: soon as he comes.”
line 91: 
line 92: “I see no occasion for that. You and the girls may go, or you may send
line 93: them by themselves, which perhaps will be still better, for as you are
line 94: as handsome as any of them, Mr. Bingley may like you the best of the
line 95: party.”
line 96: 
line 97: “My dear, you flatter me. I certainly _have_ had my share of beauty, but
line 98: I do not pretend to be anything extraordinary now. When a woman has five
line 99: grown-up daughters, she ought to give over thinking of her own beauty.”
line 100: 
line 101: “In such cases, a woman has not often much beauty to think of.”
line 102: 
line 103: “But, my dear, you must indeed go and see Mr. Bingley when he comes into
line 104: the neighbourhood.”
line 105: 
line 106: “It is more than I engage for, I assure you.”
line 107: 
line 108: “But consider your daughters. Only think what an establishment it would
line 109: be for one of them. Sir William and Lady Lucas are determined to
line 110: go, merely on that account, for in general, you know, they visit no
line 111: newcomers. Indeed you must go, for it will be impossible for _us_ to
line 112: visit him if you do not.”
line 113: 
line 114: “You are over-scrupulous, surely. I dare say Mr. Bingley will be very
line 115: glad to see you; and I will send a few lines by you to assure him of my
line 116: hearty consent to his marrying whichever he chooses of the girls; though
line 117: I must throw in a good word for my little Lizzy.”
line 118: 
line 119: “I desire you will do no such thing. Lizzy is not a bit better than the
line 120: others; and I am sure she is not half so handsome as Jane, nor half so
line 121: good-humoured as Lydia. But you are always giving _her_ the preference.”
line 122: 
line 123: “They have none of them much to recommend them,” replied he; “they are
line 124: all silly and ignorant like other girls; but Lizzy has something more of
line 125: quickness than her sisters.”
line 126: 
line 127: “Mr. Bennet, how _can_ you abuse your own children in such a way? You
line 128: take delight in vexing me. You have no compassion for my poor nerves.”
line 129: 
line 130: “You mistake me, my dear. I have a high respect for your nerves. They
line 131: are my old friends. I have heard you mention them with consideration
line 132: these last twenty years at least.”
line 133: 
line 134: “Ah, you do not know what I suffer.”
line 135: 
line 136: “But I hope you will get over it, and live to see many young men of four
line 137: thousand a year come into the neighbourhood.”
line 138: 
line 139: “It will be no use to us, if twenty such should come, since you will not
line 140: visit them.”
line 141: 
line 142: “Depend upon it, my dear, that when there are twenty, I will visit them
line 143: all.”
line 144: 
line 145: Mr. Bennet was so odd a mixture of quick parts, sarcastic humour,
line 146: reserve, and caprice, that the experience of three-and-twenty years had
line 147: been insufficient to make his wife understand his character. _Her_ mind
line 148: was less difficult to develop. She was a woman of mean understanding,
line 149: little information, and uncertain temper. When she was discontented,
line 150: she fancied herself nervous. The business of her life was to get her
line 151: daughters married; its solace was visiting and news.
line 152: 
line 153: 
line 154: 
line 155: Chapter 2
line 156: 
line 157: 
line 158: Mr. Bennet was among the earliest of those who waited on Mr. Bingley. He
line 159: had always intended to visit him, though to the last always assuring
line 160: his wife that he should not go; and till the evening after the visit was
line 161: paid she had no knowledge of it. It was then disclosed in the following
line 162: manner. Observing his second daughter employed in trimming a hat, he
line 163: suddenly addressed her with:
line 164: 
line 165: “I hope Mr. Bingley will like it, Lizzy.”
line 166: 
line 167: “We are not in a way to know _what_ Mr. Bingley likes,” said her mother
line 168: resentfully, “since we are not to visit.”
line 169: 
line 170: “But you forget, mamma,” said Elizabeth, “that we shall meet him at the
line 171: assemblies, and that Mrs. Long promised to introduce him.”
line 172: 
line 173: “I do not believe Mrs. Long will do any such thing. She has two nieces
line 174: of her own. She is a selfish, hypocritical woman, and I have no opinion
line 175: of her.”
line 176: 
line 177: “No more have I,” said Mr. Bennet; “and I am glad to find that you do
line 178: not depend on her serving you.”
line 179: 
line 180: Mrs. Bennet deigned not to make any reply, but, unable to contain
line 181: herself, began scolding one of her daughters.
line 182: 
line 183: “Don't keep coughing so, Kitty, for Heaven's sake! Have a little
line 184: compassion on my nerves. You tear them to pieces.”
line 185: 
line 186: “Kitty has no discretion in her coughs,” said her father; “she times
line 187: them ill.”
line 188: 
line 189: “I do not cough for my own amusement,” replied Kitty fretfully. “When is
line 190: your next ball to be, Lizzy?”
line 191: 
line 192: “To-morrow fortnight.”
line 193: 
line 194: “Aye, so it is,” cried her mother, “and Mrs. Long does not come back
line 195: till the day before; so it will be impossible for her to introduce him,
line 196: for she will not know him herself.”
line 197: 
line 198: “Then, my dear, you may have the advantage of your friend, and introduce
line 199: Mr. Bingley to _her_.”
line 200: 
line 201: “Impossible, Mr. Bennet, impossible, when I am not acquainted with him
line 202: myself; how can you be so teasing?”
line 203: 
line 204: “I honour your circumspection. A fortnight's acquaintance is certainly
line 205: very little. One cannot know what a man really is by the end of a
line 206: fortnight. But if _we_ do not venture somebody else will; and after all,
line 207: Mrs. Long and her neices must stand their chance; and, therefore, as
line 208: she will think it an act of kindness, if you decline the office, I will
line 209: take it on myself.”
line 210: 
line 211: The girls stared at their father. Mrs. Bennet said only, “Nonsense,
line 212: nonsense!”
line 213: 
line 214: “What can be the meaning of that emphatic exclamation?” cried he. “Do
line 215: you consider the forms of introduction, and the stress that is laid on
line 216: them, as nonsense? I cannot quite agree with you _there_. What say you,
line 217: Mary? For you are a young lady of deep reflection, I know, and read
line 218: great books and make extracts.”
line 219: 
line 220: Mary wished to say something sensible, but knew not how.
line 221: 
line 222: “While Mary is adjusting her ideas,” he continued, “let us return to Mr.
line 223: Bingley.”
line 224: 
line 225: “I am sick of Mr. Bingley,” cried his wife.
line 226: 
line 227: “I am sorry to hear _that_; but why did not you tell me that before? If
line 228: I had known as much this morning I certainly would not have called
line 229: on him. It is very unlucky; but as I have actually paid the visit, we
line 230: cannot escape the acquaintance now.”
line 231: 
line 232: The astonishment of the ladies was just what he wished; that of Mrs.
line 233: Bennet perhaps surpassing the rest; though, when the first tumult of joy
line 234: was over, she began to declare that it was what she had expected all the
line 235: while.
line 236: 
line 237: “How good it was in you, my dear Mr. Bennet! But I knew I should
line 238: persuade you at last. I was sure you loved your girls too well to
line 239: neglect such an acquaintance. Well, how pleased I am! and it is such a
line 240: good joke, too, that you should have gone this morning and never said a
line 241: word about it till now.”
line 242: 
line 243: “Now, Kitty, you may cough as much as you choose,” said Mr. Bennet; and,
line 244: as he spoke, he left the room, fatigued with the raptures of his wife.
line 245: 
line 246: “What an excellent father you have, girls!” said she, when the door was
line 247: shut. “I do not know how you will ever make him amends for his kindness;
line 248: or me, either, for that matter. At our time of life it is not so
line 249: pleasant, I can tell you, to be making new acquaintances every day; but
line 250: for your sakes, we would do anything. Lydia, my love, though you _are_
line 251: the youngest, I dare say Mr. Bingley will dance with you at the next
line 252: ball.”
line 253: 
line 254: “Oh!” said Lydia stoutly, “I am not afraid; for though I _am_ the
line 255: youngest, I'm the tallest.”
line 256: 
line 257: The rest of the evening was spent in conjecturing how soon he would
line 258: return Mr. Bennet's visit, and determining when they should ask him to
line 259: dinner.
line 260: 
line 261: 
line 262: 
line 263: Chapter 3
line 264: 
line 265: 
line 266: Not all that Mrs. Bennet, however, with the assistance of her five
line 267: daughters, could ask on the subject, was sufficient to draw from her
line 268: husband any satisfactory description of Mr. Bingley. They attacked him
line 269: in various ways--with barefaced questions, ingenious suppositions, and
line 270: distant surmises; but he eluded the skill of them all, and they were at
line 271: last obliged to accept the second-hand intelligence of their neighbour,
line 272: Lady Lucas. Her report was highly favourable. Sir William had been
line 273: delighted with him. He was quite young, wonderfully handsome, extremely
line 274: agreeable, and, to crown the whole, he meant to be at the next assembly
line 275: with a large party. Nothing could be more delightful! To be fond of
line 276: dancing was a certain step towards falling in love; and very lively
line 277: hopes of Mr. Bingley's heart were entertained.
line 278: 
line 279: “If I can but see one of my daughters happily settled at Netherfield,”
line 280:  said Mrs. Bennet to her husband, “and all the others equally well
line 281: married, I shall have nothing to wish for.”
line 282: 
line 283: In a few days Mr. Bingley returned Mr. Bennet's visit, and sat about
line 284: ten minutes with him in his library. He had entertained hopes of being
line 285: admitted to a sight of the young ladies, of whose beauty he had
line 286: heard much; but he saw only the father. The ladies were somewhat more
line 287: fortunate, for they had the advantage of ascertaining from an upper
line 288: window that he wore a blue coat, and rode a black horse.
line 289: 
line 290: An invitation to dinner was soon afterwards dispatched; and already
line 291: had Mrs. Bennet planned the courses that were to do credit to her
line 292: housekeeping, when an answer arrived which deferred it all. Mr. Bingley
line 293: was obliged to be in town the following day, and, consequently, unable
line 294: to accept the honour of their invitation, etc. Mrs. Bennet was quite
line 295: disconcerted. She could not imagine what business he could have in town
line 296: so soon after his arrival in Hertfordshire; and she began to fear that
line 297: he might be always flying about from one place to another, and never
line 298: settled at Netherfield as he ought to be. Lady Lucas quieted her fears
line 299: a little by starting the idea of his being gone to London only to get
line 300: a large party for the ball; and a report soon followed that Mr. Bingley
line 301: was to bring twelve ladies and seven gentlemen with him to the assembly.
line 302: The girls grieved over such a number of ladies, but were comforted the
line 303: day before the ball by hearing, that instead of twelve he brought only
line 304: six with him from London--his five sisters and a cousin. And when
line 305: the party entered the assembly room it consisted of only five
line 306: altogether--Mr. Bingley, his two sisters, the husband of the eldest, and
line 307: another young man.
line 308: 
line 309: Mr. Bingley was good-looking and gentlemanlike; he had a pleasant
line 310: countenance, and easy, unaffected manners. His sisters were fine women,
line 311: with an air of decided fashion. His brother-in-law, Mr. Hurst, merely
line 312: looked the gentleman; but his friend Mr. Darcy soon drew the attention
line 313: of the room by his fine, tall person, handsome features, noble mien, and
line 314: the report which was in general circulation within five minutes
line 315: after his entrance, of his having ten thousand a year. The gentlemen
line 316: pronounced him to be a fine figure of a man, the ladies declared he
line 317: was much handsomer than Mr. Bingley, and he was looked at with great
line 318: admiration for about half the evening, till his manners gave a disgust
line 319: which turned the tide of his popularity; for he was discovered to be
line 320: proud; to be above his company, and above being pleased; and not all
line 321: his large estate in Derbyshire could then save him from having a most
line 322: forbidding, disagreeable countenance, and being unworthy to be compared
line 323: with his friend.
line 324: 
line 325: Mr. Bingley had soon made himself acquainted with all the principal
line 326: people in the room; he was lively and unreserved, danced every dance,
line 327: was angry that the ball closed so early, and talked of giving
line 328: one himself at Netherfield. Such amiable qualities must speak for
line 329: themselves. What a contrast between him and his friend! Mr. Darcy danced
line 330: only once with Mrs. Hurst and once with Miss Bingley, declined being
line 331: introduced to any other lady, and spent the rest of the evening in
line 332: walking about the room, speaking occasionally to one of his own party.
line 333: His character was decided. He was the proudest, most disagreeable man
line 334: in the world, and everybody hoped that he would never come there again.
line 335: Amongst the most violent against him was Mrs. Bennet, whose dislike of
line 336: his general behaviour was sharpened into particular resentment by his
line 337: having slighted one of her daughters.
line 338: 
line 339: Elizabeth Bennet had been obliged, by the scarcity of gentlemen, to sit
line 340: down for two dances; and during part of that time, Mr. Darcy had been
line 341: standing near enough for her to hear a conversation between him and Mr.
line 342: Bingley, who came from the dance for a few minutes, to press his friend
line 343: to join it.
line 344: 
line 345: “Come, Darcy,” said he, “I must have you dance. I hate to see you
line 346: standing about by yourself in this stupid manner. You had much better
line 347: dance.”
line 348: 
line 349: “I certainly shall not. You know how I detest it, unless I am
line 350: particularly acquainted with my partner. At such an assembly as this
line 351: it would be insupportable. Your sisters are engaged, and there is not
line 352: another woman in the room whom it would not be a punishment to me to
line 353: stand up with.”
line 354: 
line 355: “I would not be so fastidious as you are,” cried Mr. Bingley, “for a
line 356: kingdom! Upon my honour, I never met with so many pleasant girls in
line 357: my life as I have this evening; and there are several of them you see
line 358: uncommonly pretty.”
line 359: 
line 360: “_You_ are dancing with the only handsome girl in the room,” said Mr.
line 361: Darcy, looking at the eldest Miss Bennet.
line 362: 
line 363: “Oh! She is the most beautiful creature I ever beheld! But there is one
line 364: of her sisters sitting down just behind you, who is very pretty, and I
line 365: dare say very agreeable. Do let me ask my partner to introduce you.”
line 366: 
line 367: “Which do you mean?” and turning round he looked for a moment at
line 368: Elizabeth, till catching her eye, he withdrew his own and coldly said:
line 369: “She is tolerable, but not handsome enough to tempt _me_; I am in no
line 370: humour at present to give consequence to young ladies who are slighted
line 371: by other men. You had better return to your partner and enjoy her
line 372: smiles, for you are wasting your time with me.”
line 373: 
line 374: Mr. Bingley followed his advice. Mr. Darcy walked off; and Elizabeth
line 375: remained with no very cordial feelings toward him. She told the story,
line 376: however, with great spirit among her friends; for she had a lively,
line 377: playful disposition, which delighted in anything ridiculous.
line 378: 
line 379: The evening altogether passed off pleasantly to the whole family. Mrs.
line 380: Bennet had seen her eldest daughter much admired by the Netherfield
line 381: party. Mr. Bingley had danced with her twice, and she had been
line 382: distinguished by his sisters. Jane was as much gratified by this as
line 383: her mother could be, though in a quieter way. Elizabeth felt Jane's
line 384: pleasure. Mary had heard herself mentioned to Miss Bingley as the most
line 385: accomplished girl in the neighbourhood; and Catherine and Lydia had been
line 386: fortunate enough never to be without partners, which was all that they
line 387: had yet learnt to care for at a ball. They returned, therefore, in good
line 388: spirits to Longbourn, the village where they lived, and of which they
line 389: were the principal inhabitants. They found Mr. Bennet still up. With
line 390: a book he was regardless of time; and on the present occasion he had a
line 391: good deal of curiosity as to the event of an evening which had raised
line 392: such splendid expectations. He had rather hoped that his wife's views on
line 393: the stranger would be disappointed; but he soon found out that he had a
line 394: different story to hear.
line 395: 
line 396: “Oh! my dear Mr. Bennet,” as she entered the room, “we have had a most
line 397: delightful evening, a most excellent ball. I wish you had been there.
line 398: Jane was so admired, nothing could be like it. Everybody said how well
line 399: she looked; and Mr. Bingley thought her quite beautiful, and danced with
line 400: her twice! Only think of _that_, my dear; he actually danced with her
line 401: twice! and she was the only creature in the room that he asked a second
line 402: time. First of all, he asked Miss Lucas. I was so vexed to see him stand
line 403: up with her! But, however, he did not admire her at all; indeed, nobody
line 404: can, you know; and he seemed quite struck with Jane as she was going
line 405: down the dance. So he inquired who she was, and got introduced, and
line 406: asked her for the two next. Then the two third he danced with Miss King,
line 407: and the two fourth with Maria Lucas, and the two fifth with Jane again,
line 408: and the two sixth with Lizzy, and the _Boulanger_--”
line 409: 
line 410: “If he had had any compassion for _me_,” cried her husband impatiently,
line 411: “he would not have danced half so much! For God's sake, say no more of
line 412: his partners. Oh that he had sprained his ankle in the first dance!”
line 413: 
line 414: “Oh! my dear, I am quite delighted with him. He is so excessively
line 415: handsome! And his sisters are charming women. I never in my life saw
line 416: anything more elegant than their dresses. I dare say the lace upon Mrs.
line 417: Hurst's gown--”
line 418: 
line 419: Here she was interrupted again. Mr. Bennet protested against any
line 420: description of finery. She was therefore obliged to seek another branch
line 421: of the subject, and related, with much bitterness of spirit and some
line 422: exaggeration, the shocking rudeness of Mr. Darcy.
line 423: 
line 424: “But I can assure you,” she added, “that Lizzy does not lose much by not
line 425: suiting _his_ fancy; for he is a most disagreeable, horrid man, not at
line 426: all worth pleasing. So high and so conceited that there was no enduring
line 427: him! He walked here, and he walked there, fancying himself so very
line 428: great! Not handsome enough to dance with! I wish you had been there, my
line 429: dear, to have given him one of your set-downs. I quite detest the man.”
line 430: 
line 431: 
line 432: 
line 433: Chapter 4
line 434: 
line 435: 
line 436: When Jane and Elizabeth were alone, the former, who had been cautious in
line 437: her praise of Mr. Bingley before, expressed to her sister just how very
line 438: much she admired him.
line 439: 
line 440: “He is just what a young man ought to be,” said she, “sensible,
line 441: good-humoured, lively; and I never saw such happy manners!--so much
line 442: ease, with such perfect good breeding!”
line 443: 
line 444: “He is also handsome,” replied Elizabeth, “which a young man ought
line 445: likewise to be, if he possibly can. His character is thereby complete.”
line 446: 
line 447: “I was very much flattered by his asking me to dance a second time. I
line 448: did not expect such a compliment.”
line 449: 
line 450: “Did not you? I did for you. But that is one great difference between
line 451: us. Compliments always take _you_ by surprise, and _me_ never. What
line 452: could be more natural than his asking you again? He could not help
line 453: seeing that you were about five times as pretty as every other woman
line 454: in the room. No thanks to his gallantry for that. Well, he certainly is
line 455: very agreeable, and I give you leave to like him. You have liked many a
line 456: stupider person.”
line 457: 
line 458: “Dear Lizzy!”
line 459: 
line 460: “Oh! you are a great deal too apt, you know, to like people in general.
line 461: You never see a fault in anybody. All the world are good and agreeable
line 462: in your eyes. I never heard you speak ill of a human being in your
line 463: life.”
line 464: 
line 465: “I would not wish to be hasty in censuring anyone; but I always speak
line 466: what I think.”
line 467: 
line 468: “I know you do; and it is _that_ which makes the wonder. With _your_
line 469: good sense, to be so honestly blind to the follies and nonsense of
line 470: others! Affectation of candour is common enough--one meets with it
line 471: everywhere. But to be candid without ostentation or design--to take the
line 472: good of everybody's character and make it still better, and say nothing
line 473: of the bad--belongs to you alone. And so you like this man's sisters,
line 474: too, do you? Their manners are not equal to his.”
line 475: 
line 476: “Certainly not--at first. But they are very pleasing women when you
line 477: converse with them. Miss Bingley is to live with her brother, and keep
line 478: his house; and I am much mistaken if we shall not find a very charming
line 479: neighbour in her.”
line 480: 
line 481: Elizabeth listened in silence, but was not convinced; their behaviour at
line 482: the assembly had not been calculated to please in general; and with more
line 483: quickness of observation and less pliancy of temper than her sister,
line 484: and with a judgement too unassailed by any attention to herself, she
line 485: was very little disposed to approve them. They were in fact very fine
line 486: ladies; not deficient in good humour when they were pleased, nor in the
line 487: power of making themselves agreeable when they chose it, but proud and
line 488: conceited. They were rather handsome, had been educated in one of the
line 489: first private seminaries in town, had a fortune of twenty thousand
line 490: pounds, were in the habit of spending more than they ought, and of
line 491: associating with people of rank, and were therefore in every respect
line 492: entitled to think well of themselves, and meanly of others. They were of
line 493: a respectable family in the north of England; a circumstance more deeply
line 494: impressed on their memories than that their brother's fortune and their
line 495: own had been acquired by trade.
line 496: 
line 497: Mr. Bingley inherited property to the amount of nearly a hundred
line 498: thousand pounds from his father, who had intended to purchase an
line 499: estate, but did not live to do it. Mr. Bingley intended it likewise, and
line 500: sometimes made choice of his county; but as he was now provided with a
line 501: good house and the liberty of a manor, it was doubtful to many of those
line 502: who best knew the easiness of his temper, whether he might not spend the
line 503: remainder of his days at Netherfield, and leave the next generation to
line 504: purchase.
line 505: 
line 506: His sisters were anxious for his having an estate of his own; but,
line 507: though he was now only established as a tenant, Miss Bingley was by no
line 508: means unwilling to preside at his table--nor was Mrs. Hurst, who had
line 509: married a man of more fashion than fortune, less disposed to consider
line 510: his house as her home when it suited her. Mr. Bingley had not been of
line 511: age two years, when he was tempted by an accidental recommendation
line 512: to look at Netherfield House. He did look at it, and into it for
line 513: half-an-hour--was pleased with the situation and the principal
line 514: rooms, satisfied with what the owner said in its praise, and took it
line 515: immediately.
line 516: 
line 517: Between him and Darcy there was a very steady friendship, in spite of
line 518: great opposition of character. Bingley was endeared to Darcy by the
line 519: easiness, openness, and ductility of his temper, though no disposition
line 520: could offer a greater contrast to his own, and though with his own he
line 521: never appeared dissatisfied. On the strength of Darcy's regard, Bingley
line 522: had the firmest reliance, and of his judgement the highest opinion.
line 523: In understanding, Darcy was the superior. Bingley was by no means
line 524: deficient, but Darcy was clever. He was at the same time haughty,
line 525: reserved, and fastidious, and his manners, though well-bred, were not
line 526: inviting. In that respect his friend had greatly the advantage. Bingley
line 527: was sure of being liked wherever he appeared, Darcy was continually
line 528: giving offense.
line 529: 
line 530: The manner in which they spoke of the Meryton assembly was sufficiently
line 531: characteristic. Bingley had never met with more pleasant people or
line 532: prettier girls in his life; everybody had been most kind and attentive
line 533: to him; there had been no formality, no stiffness; he had soon felt
line 534: acquainted with all the room; and, as to Miss Bennet, he could not
line 535: conceive an angel more beautiful. Darcy, on the contrary, had seen a
line 536: collection of people in whom there was little beauty and no fashion, for
line 537: none of whom he had felt the smallest interest, and from none received
line 538: either attention or pleasure. Miss Bennet he acknowledged to be pretty,
line 539: but she smiled too much.
line 540: 
line 541: Mrs. Hurst and her sister allowed it to be so--but still they admired
line 542: her and liked her, and pronounced her to be a sweet girl, and one
line 543: whom they would not object to know more of. Miss Bennet was therefore
line 544: established as a sweet girl, and their brother felt authorized by such
line 545: commendation to think of her as he chose.
line 546: 
line 547: 
line 548: 
line 549: Chapter 5
line 550: 
line 551: 
line 552: Within a short walk of Longbourn lived a family with whom the Bennets
line 553: were particularly intimate. Sir William Lucas had been formerly in trade
line 554: in Meryton, where he had made a tolerable fortune, and risen to the
line 555: honour of knighthood by an address to the king during his mayoralty.
line 556: The distinction had perhaps been felt too strongly. It had given him a
line 557: disgust to his business, and to his residence in a small market town;
line 558: and, in quitting them both, he had removed with his family to a house
line 559: about a mile from Meryton, denominated from that period Lucas Lodge,
line 560: where he could think with pleasure of his own importance, and,
line 561: unshackled by business, occupy himself solely in being civil to all
line 562: the world. For, though elated by his rank, it did not render him
line 563: supercilious; on the contrary, he was all attention to everybody. By
line 564: nature inoffensive, friendly, and obliging, his presentation at St.
line 565: James's had made him courteous.
line 566: 
line 567: Lady Lucas was a very good kind of woman, not too clever to be a
line 568: valuable neighbour to Mrs. Bennet. They had several children. The eldest
line 569: of them, a sensible, intelligent young woman, about twenty-seven, was
line 570: Elizabeth's intimate friend.
line 571: 
line 572: That the Miss Lucases and the Miss Bennets should meet to talk over
line 573: a ball was absolutely necessary; and the morning after the assembly
line 574: brought the former to Longbourn to hear and to communicate.
line 575: 
line 576: “_You_ began the evening well, Charlotte,” said Mrs. Bennet with civil
line 577: self-command to Miss Lucas. “_You_ were Mr. Bingley's first choice.”
line 578: 
line 579: “Yes; but he seemed to like his second better.”
line 580: 
line 581: “Oh! you mean Jane, I suppose, because he danced with her twice. To be
line 582: sure that _did_ seem as if he admired her--indeed I rather believe he
line 583: _did_--I heard something about it--but I hardly know what--something
line 584: about Mr. Robinson.”
line 585: 
line 586: “Perhaps you mean what I overheard between him and Mr. Robinson; did not
line 587: I mention it to you? Mr. Robinson's asking him how he liked our Meryton
line 588: assemblies, and whether he did not think there were a great many
line 589: pretty women in the room, and _which_ he thought the prettiest? and his
line 590: answering immediately to the last question: 'Oh! the eldest Miss Bennet,

line 592: 
line 593: “Upon my word! Well, that is very decided indeed--that does seem as

line 595: 
line 596: “_My_ overhearings were more to the purpose than _yours_, Eliza,” said

line 598: is he?--poor Eliza!--to be only just _tolerable_.”
line 599: 
line 600: “I beg you would not put it into Lizzy's head to be vexed by his
line 601: ill-treatment, for he is such a disagreeable man, that it would be quite
line 602: a misfortune to be liked by him. Mrs. Long told me last night that he
line 603: sat close to her for half-an-hour without once opening his lips.”
line 604: 
line 605: “Are you quite sure, ma'am?--is not there a little mistake?” said Jane.
line 606: “I certainly saw Mr. Darcy speaking to her.”
line 607: 
line 608: “Aye--because she asked him at last how he liked Netherfield, and he
line 609: could not help answering her; but she said he seemed quite angry at
line 610: being spoke to.”
line 611: 
line 612: “Miss Bingley told me,” said Jane, “that he never speaks much,
line 613: unless among his intimate acquaintances. With _them_ he is remarkably
line 614: agreeable.”
line 615: 
line 616: “I do not believe a word of it, my dear. If he had been so very
line 617: agreeable, he would have talked to Mrs. Long. But I can guess how it
line 618: was; everybody says that he is eat up with pride, and I dare say he had
line 619: heard somehow that Mrs. Long does not keep a carriage, and had come to
line 620: the ball in a hack chaise.”
line 621: 
line 622: “I do not mind his not talking to Mrs. Long,” said Miss Lucas, “but I
line 623: wish he had danced with Eliza.”
line 624: 
line 625: “Another time, Lizzy,” said her mother, “I would not dance with _him_,
line 626: if I were you.”
line 627: 
line 628: “I believe, ma'am, I may safely promise you _never_ to dance with him.”
line 629: 
line 630: “His pride,” said Miss Lucas, “does not offend _me_ so much as pride
line 631: often does, because there is an excuse for it. One cannot wonder that so
line 632: very fine a young man, with family, fortune, everything in his favour,
line 633: should think highly of himself. If I may so express it, he has a _right_
line 634: to be proud.”
line 635: 
line 636: “That is very true,” replied Elizabeth, “and I could easily forgive
line 637: _his_ pride, if he had not mortified _mine_.”
line 638: 
line 639: “Pride,” observed Mary, who piqued herself upon the solidity of her
line 640: reflections, “is a very common failing, I believe. By all that I have
line 641: ever read, I am convinced that it is very common indeed; that human
line 642: nature is particularly prone to it, and that there are very few of us
line 643: who do not cherish a feeling of self-complacency on the score of some
line 644: quality or other, real or imaginary. Vanity and pride are different
line 645: things, though the words are often used synonymously. A person may
line 646: be proud without being vain. Pride relates more to our opinion of
line 647: ourselves, vanity to what we would have others think of us.”
line 648: 
line 649: “If I were as rich as Mr. Darcy,” cried a young Lucas, who came with
line 650: his sisters, “I should not care how proud I was. I would keep a pack of
line 651: foxhounds, and drink a bottle of wine a day.”
line 652: 
line 653: “Then you would drink a great deal more than you ought,” said Mrs.
line 654: Bennet; “and if I were to see you at it, I should take away your bottle
line 655: directly.”
line 656: 
line 657: The boy protested that she should not; she continued to declare that she
line 658: would, and the argument ended only with the visit.
line 659: 
line 660: 
line 661: 
line 662: Chapter 6
line 663: 
line 664: 
line 665: The ladies of Longbourn soon waited on those of Netherfield. The visit
line 666: was soon returned in due form. Miss Bennet's pleasing manners grew on
line 667: the goodwill of Mrs. Hurst and Miss Bingley; and though the mother was
line 668: found to be intolerable, and the younger sisters not worth speaking to,
line 669: a wish of being better acquainted with _them_ was expressed towards
line 670: the two eldest. By Jane, this attention was received with the greatest
line 671: pleasure, but Elizabeth still saw superciliousness in their treatment
line 672: of everybody, hardly excepting even her sister, and could not like them;
line 673: though their kindness to Jane, such as it was, had a value as arising in
line 674: all probability from the influence of their brother's admiration. It
line 675: was generally evident whenever they met, that he _did_ admire her and
line 676: to _her_ it was equally evident that Jane was yielding to the preference
line 677: which she had begun to entertain for him from the first, and was in a
line 678: way to be very much in love; but she considered with pleasure that it
line 679: was not likely to be discovered by the world in general, since Jane
line 680: united, with great strength of feeling, a composure of temper and a
line 681: uniform cheerfulness of manner which would guard her from the suspicions
line 682: of the impertinent. She mentioned this to her friend Miss Lucas.
line 683: 
line 684: “It may perhaps be pleasant,” replied Charlotte, “to be able to impose
line 685: on the public in such a case; but it is sometimes a disadvantage to be
line 686: so very guarded. If a woman conceals her affection with the same skill
line 687: from the object of it, she may lose the opportunity of fixing him; and
line 688: it will then be but poor consolation to believe the world equally in
line 689: the dark. There is so much of gratitude or vanity in almost every
line 690: attachment, that it is not safe to leave any to itself. We can all
line 691: _begin_ freely--a slight preference is natural enough; but there are
line 692: very few of us who have heart enough to be really in love without
line 693: encouragement. In nine cases out of ten a women had better show _more_
line 694: affection than she feels. Bingley likes your sister undoubtedly; but he
line 695: may never do more than like her, if she does not help him on.”
line 696: 
line 697: “But she does help him on, as much as her nature will allow. If I can
line 698: perceive her regard for him, he must be a simpleton, indeed, not to
line 699: discover it too.”
line 700: 
line 701: “Remember, Eliza, that he does not know Jane's disposition as you do.”
line 702: 
line 703: “But if a woman is partial to a man, and does not endeavour to conceal
line 704: it, he must find it out.”
line 705: 
line 706: “Perhaps he must, if he sees enough of her. But, though Bingley and Jane
line 707: meet tolerably often, it is never for many hours together; and, as they
line 708: always see each other in large mixed parties, it is impossible that
line 709: every moment should be employed in conversing together. Jane should
line 710: therefore make the most of every half-hour in which she can command his
line 711: attention. When she is secure of him, there will be more leisure for
line 712: falling in love as much as she chooses.”
line 713: 
line 714: “Your plan is a good one,” replied Elizabeth, “where nothing is in
line 715: question but the desire of being well married, and if I were determined
line 716: to get a rich husband, or any husband, I dare say I should adopt it. But
line 717: these are not Jane's feelings; she is not acting by design. As yet,
line 718: she cannot even be certain of the degree of her own regard nor of its
line 719: reasonableness. She has known him only a fortnight. She danced four
line 720: dances with him at Meryton; she saw him one morning at his own house,
line 721: and has since dined with him in company four times. This is not quite
line 722: enough to make her understand his character.”
line 723: 
line 724: “Not as you represent it. Had she merely _dined_ with him, she might
line 725: only have discovered whether he had a good appetite; but you must
line 726: remember that four evenings have also been spent together--and four
line 727: evenings may do a great deal.”
line 728: 
line 729: “Yes; these four evenings have enabled them to ascertain that they
line 730: both like Vingt-un better than Commerce; but with respect to any other
line 731: leading characteristic, I do not imagine that much has been unfolded.”
line 732: 
line 733: “Well,” said Charlotte, “I wish Jane success with all my heart; and
line 734: if she were married to him to-morrow, I should think she had as good a
line 735: chance of happiness as if she were to be studying his character for a
line 736: twelvemonth. Happiness in marriage is entirely a matter of chance. If
line 737: the dispositions of the parties are ever so well known to each other or
line 738: ever so similar beforehand, it does not advance their felicity in the
line 739: least. They always continue to grow sufficiently unlike afterwards to
line 740: have their share of vexation; and it is better to know as little as
line 741: possible of the defects of the person with whom you are to pass your
line 742: life.”
line 743: 
line 744: “You make me laugh, Charlotte; but it is not sound. You know it is not
line 745: sound, and that you would never act in this way yourself.”
line 746: 
line 747: Occupied in observing Mr. Bingley's attentions to her sister, Elizabeth
line 748: was far from suspecting that she was herself becoming an object of some
line 749: interest in the eyes of his friend. Mr. Darcy had at first scarcely
line 750: allowed her to be pretty; he had looked at her without admiration at the
line 751: ball; and when they next met, he looked at her only to criticise. But no
line 752: sooner had he made it clear to himself and his friends that she hardly
line 753: had a good feature in her face, than he began to find it was rendered
line 754: uncommonly intelligent by the beautiful expression of her dark eyes. To
line 755: this discovery succeeded some others equally mortifying. Though he had
line 756: detected with a critical eye more than one failure of perfect symmetry
line 757: in her form, he was forced to acknowledge her figure to be light and
line 758: pleasing; and in spite of his asserting that her manners were not those
line 759: of the fashionable world, he was caught by their easy playfulness. Of
line 760: this she was perfectly unaware; to her he was only the man who made
line 761: himself agreeable nowhere, and who had not thought her handsome enough
line 762: to dance with.
line 763: 
line 764: He began to wish to know more of her, and as a step towards conversing
line 765: with her himself, attended to her conversation with others. His doing so
line 766: drew her notice. It was at Sir William Lucas's, where a large party were
line 767: assembled.
line 768: 
line 769: “What does Mr. Darcy mean,” said she to Charlotte, “by listening to my
line 770: conversation with Colonel Forster?”
line 771: 
line 772: “That is a question which Mr. Darcy only can answer.”
line 773: 
line 774: “But if he does it any more I shall certainly let him know that I see
line 775: what he is about. He has a very satirical eye, and if I do not begin by
line 776: being impertinent myself, I shall soon grow afraid of him.”
line 777: 
line 778: On his approaching them soon afterwards, though without seeming to have
line 779: any intention of speaking, Miss Lucas defied her friend to mention such
line 780: a subject to him; which immediately provoking Elizabeth to do it, she
line 781: turned to him and said:
line 782: 
line 783: “Did you not think, Mr. Darcy, that I expressed myself uncommonly
line 784: well just now, when I was teasing Colonel Forster to give us a ball at
line 785: Meryton?”
line 786: 
line 787: “With great energy; but it is always a subject which makes a lady
line 788: energetic.”
line 789: 
line 790: “You are severe on us.”
line 791: 
line 792: “It will be _her_ turn soon to be teased,” said Miss Lucas. “I am going
line 793: to open the instrument, Eliza, and you know what follows.”
line 794: 
line 795: “You are a very strange creature by way of a friend!--always wanting me
line 796: to play and sing before anybody and everybody! If my vanity had taken
line 797: a musical turn, you would have been invaluable; but as it is, I would
line 798: really rather not sit down before those who must be in the habit of
line 799: hearing the very best performers.” On Miss Lucas's persevering, however,

line 801: at Mr. Darcy, “There is a fine old saying, which everybody here is of
line 802: course familiar with: 'Keep your breath to cool your porridge'; and I
line 803: shall keep mine to swell my song.”
line 804: 
line 805: Her performance was pleasing, though by no means capital. After a song
line 806: or two, and before she could reply to the entreaties of several that
line 807: she would sing again, she was eagerly succeeded at the instrument by her
line 808: sister Mary, who having, in consequence of being the only plain one in
line 809: the family, worked hard for knowledge and accomplishments, was always
line 810: impatient for display.
line 811: 
line 812: Mary had neither genius nor taste; and though vanity had given her
line 813: application, it had given her likewise a pedantic air and conceited
line 814: manner, which would have injured a higher degree of excellence than she
line 815: had reached. Elizabeth, easy and unaffected, had been listened to with
line 816: much more pleasure, though not playing half so well; and Mary, at the
line 817: end of a long concerto, was glad to purchase praise and gratitude by

line 821: 
line 822: Mr. Darcy stood near them in silent indignation at such a mode of
line 823: passing the evening, to the exclusion of all conversation, and was too

line 825: his neighbour, till Sir William thus began:
line 826: 
line 827: “What a charming amusement for young people this is, Mr. Darcy! There
line 828: is nothing like dancing after all. I consider it as one of the first
line 829: refinements of polished society.”
line 830: 
line 831: “Certainly, sir; and it has the advantage also of being in vogue amongst
line 832: the less polished societies of the world. Every savage can dance.”
line 833: 
line 834: Sir William only smiled. “Your friend performs delightfully,” he
line 835: continued after a pause, on seeing Bingley join the group; “and I doubt
line 836: not that you are an adept in the science yourself, Mr. Darcy.”
line 837: 
line 838: “You saw me dance at Meryton, I believe, sir.”
line 839: 
line 840: “Yes, indeed, and received no inconsiderable pleasure from the sight. Do
line 841: you often dance at St. James's?”
line 842: 
line 843: “Never, sir.”
line 844: 
line 845: “Do you not think it would be a proper compliment to the place?”
line 846: 
line 847: “It is a compliment which I never pay to any place if I can avoid it.”
line 848: 
line 849: “You have a house in town, I conclude?”
line 850: 
line 851: Mr. Darcy bowed.
line 852: 
line 853: “I had once had some thought of fixing in town myself--for I am fond

line 856: 
line 857: He paused in hopes of an answer; but his companion was not disposed

line 868:

line 871: 
line 872: Mr. Darcy, with grave propriety, requested to be allowed the honour of

line 875: 
line 876: “You excel so much in the dance, Miss Eliza, that it is cruel to deny

line 878: amusement in general, he can have no objection, I am sure, to oblige us

line 880: 
line 881: “Mr. Darcy is all politeness,” said Elizabeth, smiling.
line 882:

line 886: 
line 887: Elizabeth looked archly, and turned away. Her resistance had not

line 890: 
line 891: “I can guess the subject of your reverie.”
line 892: 
line 893: “I should imagine not.”
line 894:

line 897: I was never more annoyed! The insipidity, and yet the noise--the

line 900: 
line 901: “Your conjecture is totally wrong, I assure you. My mind was more
line 902: agreeably engaged. I have been meditating on the very great pleasure
line 903: which a pair of fine eyes in the face of a pretty woman can bestow.”
line 904: 
line 905: Miss Bingley immediately fixed her eyes on his face, and desired he
line 906: would tell her what lady had the credit of inspiring such reflections.
line 907: Mr. Darcy replied with great intrepidity:
line 908: 
line 909: “Miss Elizabeth Bennet.”
line 910: 
line 911: “Miss Elizabeth Bennet!” repeated Miss Bingley. “I am all astonishment.
line 912: How long has she been such a favourite?--and pray, when am I to wish you
line 913: joy?”
line 914: 
line 915: “That is exactly the question which I expected you to ask. A lady's
line 916: imagination is very rapid; it jumps from admiration to love, from love
line 917: to matrimony, in a moment. I knew you would be wishing me joy.”
line 918: 
line 919: “Nay, if you are serious about it, I shall consider the matter is
line 920: absolutely settled. You will be having a charming mother-in-law, indeed;
line 921: and, of course, she will always be at Pemberley with you.”
line 922: 
line 923: He listened to her with perfect indifference while she chose to
line 924: entertain herself in this manner; and as his composure convinced her

line 926: 
line 927: 
line 928: 
line 929: Chapter 7
line 930: 
line 931: 
line 932: Mr. Bennet's property consisted almost entirely in an estate of two
line 933: thousand a year, which, unfortunately for his daughters, was entailed,
line 934: in default of heirs male, on a distant relation; and their mother's
line 935: fortune, though ample for her situation in life, could but ill supply
line 936: the deficiency of his. Her father had been an attorney in Meryton, and
line 937: had left her four thousand pounds.
line 938: 
line 939: She had a sister married to a Mr. Phillips, who had been a clerk to
line 940: their father and succeeded him in the business, and a brother settled in
line 941: London in a respectable line of trade.
line 942: 
line 943: The village of Longbourn was only one mile from Meryton; a most
line 944: convenient distance for the young ladies, who were usually tempted
line 945: thither three or four times a week, to pay their duty to their aunt and
line 946: to a milliner's shop just over the way. The two youngest of the family,
line 947: Catherine and Lydia, were particularly frequent in these attentions;
line 948: their minds were more vacant than their sisters', and when nothing
line 949: better offered, a walk to Meryton was necessary to amuse their morning
line 950: hours and furnish conversation for the evening; and however bare of news
line 951: the country in general might be, they always contrived to learn some
line 952: from their aunt. At present, indeed, they were well supplied both with
line 953: news and happiness by the recent arrival of a militia regiment in the
line 954: neighbourhood; it was to remain the whole winter, and Meryton was the
line 955: headquarters.
line 956: 
line 957: Their visits to Mrs. Phillips were now productive of the most
line 958: interesting intelligence. Every day added something to their knowledge
line 959: of the officers' names and connections. Their lodgings were not long a
line 960: secret, and at length they began to know the officers themselves. Mr.
line 961: Phillips visited them all, and this opened to his nieces a store of
line 962: felicity unknown before. They could talk of nothing but officers; and
line 963: Mr. Bingley's large fortune, the mention of which gave animation
line 964: to their mother, was worthless in their eyes when opposed to the
line 965: regimentals of an ensign.
line 966: 
line 967: After listening one morning to their effusions on this subject, Mr.
line 968: Bennet coolly observed:
line 969: 
line 970: “From all that I can collect by your manner of talking, you must be two
line 971: of the silliest girls in the country. I have suspected it some time, but
line 972: I am now convinced.”
line 973: 
line 974: Catherine was disconcerted, and made no answer; but Lydia, with perfect
line 975: indifference, continued to express her admiration of Captain Carter,
line 976: and her hope of seeing him in the course of the day, as he was going the
line 977: next morning to London.
line 978: 
line 979: “I am astonished, my dear,” said Mrs. Bennet, “that you should be so
line 980: ready to think your own children silly. If I wished to think slightingly
line 981: of anybody's children, it should not be of my own, however.”
line 982: 
line 983: “If my children are silly, I must hope to be always sensible of it.”
line 984: 
line 985: “Yes--but as it happens, they are all of them very clever.”
line 986: 
line 987: “This is the only point, I flatter myself, on which we do not agree. I
line 988: had hoped that our sentiments coincided in every particular, but I must
line 989: so far differ from you as to think our two youngest daughters uncommonly
line 990: foolish.”
line 991: 
line 992: “My dear Mr. Bennet, you must not expect such girls to have the sense of
line 993: their father and mother. When they get to our age, I dare say they will
line 994: not think about officers any more than we do. I remember the time when
line 995: I liked a red coat myself very well--and, indeed, so I do still at my
line 996: heart; and if a smart young colonel, with five or six thousand a year,
line 997: should want one of my girls I shall not say nay to him; and I thought
line 998: Colonel Forster looked very becoming the other night at Sir William's in
line 999: his regimentals.”
line 1000: 
line 1001: “Mamma,” cried Lydia, “my aunt says that Colonel Forster and Captain
line 1002: Carter do not go so often to Miss Watson's as they did when they first
line 1003: came; she sees them now very often standing in Clarke's library.”
line 1004: 
line 1005: Mrs. Bennet was prevented replying by the entrance of the footman with
line 1006: a note for Miss Bennet; it came from Netherfield, and the servant waited
line 1007: for an answer. Mrs. Bennet's eyes sparkled with pleasure, and she was
line 1008: eagerly calling out, while her daughter read,
line 1009: 
line 1010: “Well, Jane, who is it from? What is it about? What does he say? Well,

line 1511: such a variety of people to be met with in the country as in the town,
line 1512: which you must acknowledge to be true.”
line 1513: 
line 1514: “Certainly, my dear, nobody said there were; but as to not meeting
line 1515: with many people in this neighbourhood, I believe there are few
line 1516: neighbourhoods larger. I know we dine with four-and-twenty families.”
line 1517: 
line 1518: Nothing but concern for Elizabeth could enable Bingley to keep his

line 1526: genteel and easy! He has always something to say to everybody. _That_
line 1527: is my idea of good breeding; and those persons who fancy themselves very
line 1528: important, and never open their mouths, quite mistake the matter.”
line 1529:

line 1531:

line 1549: on her, and very pretty they were.”
line 1550:

line 1557: “Of a fine, stout, healthy love it may. Everything nourishes what is

line 1574: 
line 1575: Lydia was a stout, well-grown girl of fifteen, with a fine complexion
line 1576: and good-humoured countenance; a favourite with her mother, whose
line 1577: affection had brought her into public at an early age. She had high
line 1578: animal spirits, and a sort of natural self-consequence, which the
line 1579: attention of the officers, to whom her uncle's good dinners, and her own

line 1582: abruptly reminded him of his promise; adding, that it would be the most
line 1583: shameful thing in the world if he did not keep it. His answer to this
line 1584: sudden attack was delightful to their mother's ear:
line 1585: 
line 1586: “I am perfectly ready, I assure you, to keep my engagement; and when
line 1587: your sister is recovered, you shall, if you please, name the very day of
line 1588: the ball. But you would not wish to be dancing when she is ill.”
line 1589: 
line 1590: Lydia declared herself satisfied. “Oh! yes--it would be much better to

line 1594: Forster it will be quite a shame if he does not.”
line 1595: 
line 1596: Mrs. Bennet and her daughters then departed, and Elizabeth returned

line 1599: could not be prevailed on to join in their censure of _her_, in spite of

line 1604: Chapter 10
line 1605: 
line 1606: 
line 1607: The day passed much as the day before had done. Mrs. Hurst and Miss
line 1608: Bingley had spent some hours of the morning with the invalid, who

line 1615: 
line 1616: Elizabeth took up some needlework, and was sufficiently amused in

line 1621: exactly in union with her opinion of each.
line 1622: 
line 1623: “How delighted Miss Darcy will be to receive such a letter!”

line 1626: 
line 1627: “You write uncommonly fast.”
line 1628: 
line 1629: “You are mistaken. I write rather slowly.”
line 1630: 
line 1631: “How many letters you must have occasion to write in the course of a
line 1632: year! Letters of business, too! How odious I should think them!”
line 1633:

line 1648: 
line 1649: “Tell your sister I am delighted to hear of her improvement on the harp;

line 1674:

line 1690: quickness is always prized much by the possessor, and often without any

line 1703:

line 1734:

line 1737:

line 1751:

line 1782: 
line 1783: When that business was over, he applied to Miss Bingley and Elizabeth

line 1788: 
line 1789: Mrs. Hurst sang with her sister, and while they were thus employed,

line 1799: 
line 1800: After playing some Italian songs, Miss Bingley varied the charm by
line 1801: a lively Scotch air; and soon afterwards Mr. Darcy, drawing near

line 1803:

line 1806: 
line 1807: She smiled, but made no answer. He repeated the question, with some

line 1809:

line 1813: delight in overthrowing those kind of schemes, and cheating a person of

line 1817: 
line 1818: “Indeed I do not dare.”
line 1819: 
line 1820: Elizabeth, having rather expected to affront him, was amazed at his
line 1821: gallantry; but there was a mixture of sweetness and archness in her

line 1823: had never been so bewitched by any woman as he was by her. He really
line 1824: believed, that were it not for the inferiority of her connections, he
line 1825: should be in some danger.
line 1826: 
line 1827: Miss Bingley saw, or suspected enough to be jealous; and her great
line 1828: anxiety for the recovery of her dear friend Jane received some
line 1829: assistance from her desire of getting rid of Elizabeth.
line 1830: 
line 1831: She often tried to provoke Darcy into disliking her guest, by talking of
line 1832: their supposed marriage, and planning his happiness in such an alliance.
line 1833: 
line 1834: “I hope,” said she, as they were walking together in the shrubbery

line 1836: desirable event takes place, as to the advantage of holding her tongue;
line 1837: and if you can compass it, do cure the younger girls of running after

line 1839: check that little something, bordering on conceit and impertinence,
line 1840: which your lady possesses.”
line 1841: 
line 1842: “Have you anything else to propose for my domestic felicity?”
line 1843: 
line 1844: “Oh! yes. Do let the portraits of your uncle and aunt Phillips be placed

line 1846: judge. They are in the same profession, you know, only in different

line 1849: 
line 1850: “It would not be easy, indeed, to catch their expression, but their
line 1851: colour and shape, and the eyelashes, so remarkably fine, might be

line 1853: 
line 1854: At that moment they were met from another walk by Mrs. Hurst and
line 1855: Elizabeth herself.
line 1856: 
line 1857: “I did not know that you intended to walk,” said Miss Bingley, in some

line 1859:

line 1862: 
line 1863: Then taking the disengaged arm of Mr. Darcy, she left Elizabeth to walk

line 1865: and immediately said:
line 1866: 
line 1867: “This walk is not wide enough for our party. We had better go into the
line 1868: avenue.”
line 1869: 
line 1870: But Elizabeth, who had not the least inclination to remain with them,

line 1872:

line 1876:

line 1881: 
line 1882: 
line 1883: Chapter 11
line 1884: 
line 1885: 
line 1886: When the ladies removed after dinner, Elizabeth ran up to her
line 1887: sister, and seeing her well guarded from cold, attended her into the
line 1888: drawing-room, where she was welcomed by her two friends with many
line 1889: professions of pleasure; and Elizabeth had never seen them so agreeable
line 1890: as they were during the hour which passed before the gentlemen appeared.
line 1891: Their powers of conversation were considerable. They could describe an
line 1892: entertainment with accuracy, relate an anecdote with humour, and laugh
line 1893: at their acquaintance with spirit.
line 1894: 
line 1895: But when the gentlemen entered, Jane was no longer the first object;
line 1896: Miss Bingley's eyes were instantly turned toward Darcy, and she had

line 1898: himself to Miss Bennet, with a polite congratulation; Mr. Hurst also

line 1900: and warmth remained for Bingley's salutation. He was full of joy and

line 1902: should suffer from the change of room; and she removed at his desire
line 1903: to the other side of the fireplace, that she might be further from
line 1904: the door. He then sat down by her, and talked scarcely to anyone

line 1906: delight.
line 1907: 
line 1908: When tea was over, Mr. Hurst reminded his sister-in-law of the

line 1915: and Mrs. Hurst, principally occupied in playing with her bracelets

line 1917: Bennet.
line 1918: 
line 1919: Miss Bingley's attention was quite as much engaged in watching Mr.

line 1927: enjoyment like reading! How much sooner one tires of anything than of a
line 1928: book! When I have a house of my own, I shall be miserable if I have not
line 1929: an excellent library.”
line 1930: 
line 1931: No one made any reply. She then yawned again, threw aside her book, and

line 1933: her brother mentioning a ball to Miss Bennet, she turned suddenly
line 1934: towards him and said:
line 1935: 
line 1936: “By the bye, Charles, are you really serious in meditating a dance at
line 1937: Netherfield? I would advise you, before you determine on it, to consult
line 1938: the wishes of the present party; I am much mistaken if there are
line 1939: not some among us to whom a ball would be rather a punishment than a

line 1941: 
line 1942: “If you mean Darcy,” cried her brother, “he may go to bed, if he

line 1946: 
line 1947: “I should like balls infinitely better,” she replied, “if they were
line 1948: carried on in a different manner; but there is something insufferably
line 1949: tedious in the usual process of such a meeting. It would surely be much

line 1952: 
line 1953: “Much more rational, my dear Caroline, I dare say, but it would not be
line 1954: near so much like a ball.”
line 1955: 
line 1956: Miss Bingley made no answer, and soon afterwards she got up and walked

line 1959: the desperation of her feelings, she resolved on one effort more, and,

line 1961: 
line 1962: “Miss Eliza Bennet, let me persuade you to follow my example, and take a
line 1963: turn about the room. I assure you it is very refreshing after sitting so
line 1964: long in one attitude.”
line 1965: 
line 1966: Elizabeth was surprised, but agreed to it immediately. Miss Bingley

line 1971: he could imagine but two motives for their choosing to walk up and down

line 1973: interfere. “What could he mean? She was dying to know what could be his
line 1974: meaning?”--and asked Elizabeth whether she could at all understand him?
line 1975: 
line 1976: “Not at all,” was her answer; “but depend upon it, he means to be severe

line 1979: 
line 1980: Miss Bingley, however, was incapable of disappointing Mr. Darcy in

line 1983: 
line 1984: “I have not the smallest objection to explaining them,” said he, as soon

line 1989: completely in your way, and if the second, I can admire you much better

line 1991:

line 1994:

line 1997: as you are, you must know how it is to be done.”
line 1998: 
line 1999: “But upon my honour, I do _not_. I do assure you that my intimacy has

line 2441: 
line 2442: Mr. Collins was not a sensible man, and the deficiency of nature had
line 2443: been but little assisted by education or society; the greatest part
line 2444: of his life having been spent under the guidance of an illiterate and
line 2445: miserly father; and though he belonged to one of the universities, he

line 4252: the only provision for well-educated young women of small fortune,

line 4254: preservative from want. This preservative she had now obtained; and at
line 4255: the age of twenty-seven, without having ever been handsome, she felt all
line 4256: the good luck of it. The least agreeable circumstance in the business

line 4259: and probably would blame her; and though her resolution was not to be
line 4260: shaken, her feelings must be hurt by such a disapprobation. She resolved

line 4361: was impossible for that friend to be tolerably happy in the lot she had

line 4363: 
line 4364: 
line 4365: 
line 4366: Chapter 23
line 4367: 
line 4368: 
line 4369: Elizabeth was sitting with her mother and sisters, reflecting on what

line 4381: 
line 4382: Nothing less than the complaisance of a courtier could have borne

line 4387:

line 4412: 
line 4413: Mr. Bennet's emotions were much more tranquil on the occasion, and such

line 4418:

line 4425:

line 4431:

line 4440: 
line 4441: Jane had sent Caroline an early answer to her letter, and was counting

line 4456: 
line 4457: Mr. Collins's return into Hertfordshire was no longer a matter of

line 4462: while her health was so indifferent, and lovers were of all people the
line 4463: most disagreeable. Such were the gentle murmurs of Mrs. Bennet, and

line 4466: 
line 4467: Neither Jane nor Elizabeth were comfortable on this subject. Day after

line 4473: 
line 4474: Even Elizabeth began to fear--not that Bingley was indifferent--but that

line 4479: and of his overpowering friend, assisted by the attractions of Miss
line 4480: Darcy and the amusements of London might be too much, she feared, for

line 4482:

line 4492:

line 4501: Mrs. Bennet was really in a most pitiable state. The very mention of

line 4516: “My dear, do not give way to such gloomy thoughts. Let us hope for

line 4521:

line 4541: Chapter 24
line 4542: 
line 4543: 
line 4544: Miss Bingley's letter arrived, and put an end to doubt. The very first

line 4549:

line 4559:

line 4570: been the only sacrifice, he might have been allowed to sport with it in
line 4571: whatever manner he thought best, but her sister's was involved in it, as
line 4572: she thought he must be sensible himself. It was a subject, in short,

line 4578: materially affected by the difference, her sister's situation remained

line 4580: 
line 4581: A day or two passed before Jane had courage to speak of her feelings to

line 4701:

line 4713: “your sister is crossed in love, I find. I congratulate her. Next to

line 4715: It is something to think of, and it gives her a sort of distinction

line 4717: be long outdone by Jane. Now is your time. Here are officers enough in

line 4747: 
line 4748: After a week spent in professions of love and schemes of felicity,

line 5111:

line 5129: 
line 5130: Chapter 27
line 5131: 
line 5132: 
line 5133: With no greater events than these in the Longbourn family, and otherwise

line 6567: marriage from Mr. Darcy! That he should have been in love with her for
line 6568: so many months! So much in love as to wish to marry her in spite of
line 6569: all the objections which had made him prevent his friend's marrying
line 6570: her sister, and which must appear at least with equal force in his
line 6571: own case--was almost incredible! It was gratifying to have inspired
line 6572: unconsciously so strong an affection. But his pride, his abominable
line 6573: pride--his shameless avowal of what he had done with respect to
line 6574: Jane--his unpardonable assurance in acknowledging, though he could
line 6575: not justify it, and the unfeeling manner in which he had mentioned Mr.
line 6576: Wickham, his cruelty towards whom he had not attempted to deny, soon

line 6578: a moment excited. She continued in very agitated reflections till the
line 6579: sound of Lady Catherine's carriage made her feel how unequal she was to
line 6580: encounter Charlotte's observation, and hurried her away to her room.
line 6581: 
line 6582: 
line 6583: 
line 6584: Chapter 35
line 6585: 
line 6586: 
line 6587: Elizabeth awoke the next morning to the same thoughts and meditations
line 6588: which had at length closed her eyes. She could not yet recover from the
line 6589: surprise of what had happened; it was impossible to think of anything
line 6590: else; and, totally indisposed for employment, she resolved, soon after

line 6592: directly to her favourite walk, when the recollection of Mr. Darcy's
line 6593: sometimes coming there stopped her, and instead of entering the park,
line 6594: she turned up the lane, which led farther from the turnpike-road. The
line 6595: park paling was still the boundary on one side, and she soon passed one
line 6596: of the gates into the ground.
line 6597: 
line 6598: After walking two or three times along that part of the lane, she was

line 6601: made a great difference in the country, and every day was adding to the
line 6602: verdure of the early trees. She was on the point of continuing her walk,
line 6603: when she caught a glimpse of a gentleman within the sort of grove which
line 6604: edged the park; he was moving that way; and, fearful of its being Mr.
line 6605: Darcy, she was directly retreating. But the person who advanced was now

line 6613: slight bow, turned again into the plantation, and was soon out of sight.
line 6614: 
line 6615: With no expectation of pleasure, but with the strongest curiosity,

line 6621: 
line 6622: “Be not alarmed, madam, on receiving this letter, by the apprehension

line 6624: offers which were last night so disgusting to you. I write without any
line 6625: intention of paining you, or humbling myself, by dwelling on wishes

line 6631: demand it of your justice.
line 6632: 
line 6633: “Two offenses of a very different nature, and by no means of equal
line 6634: magnitude, you last night laid to my charge. The first mentioned was,
line 6635: that, regardless of the sentiments of either, I had detached Mr. Bingley
line 6636: from your sister, and the other, that I had, in defiance of various

line 6639: wantonly to have thrown off the companion of my youth, the acknowledged

line 6642: its exertion, would be a depravity, to which the separation of two young
line 6643: persons, whose affection could be the growth of only a few weeks, could

line 6651: 
line 6652: “I had not been long in Hertfordshire, before I saw, in common with

line 6702: “The part which I acted is now to be explained. His sisters' uneasiness

line 6733: “With respect to that other, more weighty accusation, of having injured

line 6739: “Mr. Wickham is the son of a very respectable man, who had for many

line 6759: 
line 6760: “My excellent father died about five years ago; and his attachment to

line 6797: 
line 6798: “I must now mention a circumstance which I would wish to forget myself,

line 6824: 
line 6825: “This, madam, is a faithful narrative of every event in which we have

line 6847: “FITZWILLIAM DARCY”

line 6851: Chapter 36
line 6852: 
line 6853: 
line 6854: If Elizabeth, when Mr. Darcy gave her the letter, did not expect it to
line 6855: contain a renewal of his offers, she had formed no expectation at all of
line 6856: its contents. But such as they were, it may well be supposed how eagerly
line 6857: she went through them, and what a contrariety of emotion they excited.
line 6858: Her feelings as she read were scarcely to be defined. With amazement did
line 6859: she first understand that he believed any apology to be in his power;
line 6860: and steadfastly was she persuaded, that he could have no explanation
line 6861: to give, which a just sense of shame would not conceal. With a strong

line 6863: had happened at Netherfield. She read with an eagerness which hardly
line 6864: left her power of comprehension, and from impatience of knowing what the
line 6865: next sentence might bring, was incapable of attending to the sense of
line 6866: the one before her eyes. His belief of her sister's insensibility she
line 6867: instantly resolved to be false; and his account of the real, the worst
line 6868: objections to the match, made her too angry to have any wish of doing
line 6869: him justice. He expressed no regret for what he had done which satisfied
line 6870: her; his style was not penitent, but haughty. It was all pride and
line 6871: insolence.
line 6872: 
line 6873: But when this subject was succeeded by his account of Mr. Wickham--when
line 6874: she read with somewhat clearer attention a relation of events which,
line 6875: if true, must overthrow every cherished opinion of his worth, and which

line 6878: Astonishment, apprehension, and even horror, oppressed her. She wished
line 6879: to discredit it entirely, repeatedly exclaiming, “This must be false!
line 6880: This cannot be! This must be the grossest falsehood!”--and when she had
line 6881: gone through the whole letter, though scarcely knowing anything of the
line 6882: last page or two, put it hastily away, protesting that she would not
line 6883: regard it, that she would never look in it again.
line 6884: 
line 6885: In this perturbed state of mind, with thoughts that could rest on

line 6891: he had related himself; and the kindness of the late Mr. Darcy, though
line 6892: she had not before known its extent, agreed equally well with his own

line 6894: will, the difference was great. What Wickham had said of the living

line 6909: 
line 6910: The extravagance and general profligacy which he scrupled not to lay at

line 6941: 
line 6942: She perfectly remembered everything that had passed in conversation

line 6954: that he had then no reserves, no scruples in sinking Mr. Darcy's

line 6957: 
line 6958: How differently did everything now appear in which he was concerned!

line 6981: 
line 6982: She grew absolutely ashamed of herself. Of neither Darcy nor Wickham
line 6983: could she think without feeling she had been blind, partial, prejudiced,
line 6984: absurd.
line 6985: 
line 6986: “How despicably I have acted!” she cried; “I, who have prided myself
line 6987: on my discernment! I, who have valued myself on my abilities! who have
line 6988: often disdained the generous candour of my sister, and gratified
line 6989: my vanity in useless or blameable mistrust! How humiliating is this
line 6990: discovery! Yet, how just a humiliation! Had I been in love, I could
line 6991: not have been more wretchedly blind! But vanity, not love, has been my
line 6992: folly. Pleased with the preference of one, and offended by the neglect
line 6993: of the other, on the very beginning of our acquaintance, I have courted
line 6994: prepossession and ignorance, and driven reason away, where either were
line 6995: concerned. Till this moment I never knew myself.”
line 6996: 
line 6997: From herself to Jane--from Jane to Bingley, her thoughts were in a line
line 6998: which soon brought to her recollection that Mr. Darcy's explanation
line 6999: _there_ had appeared very insufficient, and she read it again. Widely
line 7000: different was the effect of a second perusal. How could she deny that
line 7001: credit to his assertions in one instance, which she had been obliged to
line 7002: give in the other? He declared himself to be totally unsuspicious of her
line 7003: sister's attachment; and she could not help remembering what Charlotte's
line 7004: opinion had always been. Neither could she deny the justice of his
line 7005: description of Jane. She felt that Jane's feelings, though fervent, were
line 7006: little displayed, and that there was a constant complacency in her air
line 7007: and manner not often united with great sensibility.
line 7008: 
line 7009: When she came to that part of the letter in which her family were
line 7010: mentioned in terms of such mortifying, yet merited reproach, her sense
line 7011: of shame was severe. The justice of the charge struck her too forcibly
line 7012: for denial, and the circumstances to which he particularly alluded as
line 7013: having passed at the Netherfield ball, and as confirming all his first
line 7014: disapprobation, could not have made a stronger impression on his mind
line 7015: than on hers.
line 7016: 
line 7017: The compliment to herself and her sister was not unfelt. It soothed,
line 7018: but it could not console her for the contempt which had thus been
line 7019: self-attracted by the rest of her family; and as she considered
line 7020: that Jane's disappointment had in fact been the work of her nearest
line 7021: relations, and reflected how materially the credit of both must be hurt
line 7022: by such impropriety of conduct, she felt depressed beyond anything she
line 7023: had ever known before.
line 7024: 
line 7025: After wandering along the lane for two hours, giving way to every
line 7026: variety of thought--re-considering events, determining probabilities,
line 7027: and reconciling herself, as well as she could, to a change so sudden and

line 7032:

line 7038: in missing him; she really rejoiced at it. Colonel Fitzwilliam was no

line 7041: 
line 7042: 
line 7043: Chapter 37
line 7044: 
line 7045: 
line 7046: The two gentlemen left Rosings the next morning, and Mr. Collins having

line 7062: Their first subject was the diminution of the Rosings party. “I assure

line 7124: 
line 7125: Lady Catherine had many other questions to ask respecting their journey,

line 7131: walk, in which she might indulge in all the delight of unpleasant
line 7132: recollections.
line 7133: 
line 7134: Mr. Darcy's letter she was in a fair way of soon knowing by heart. She

line 7137: she was still full of indignation; but when she considered how unjustly

line 7141: could not approve him; nor could she for a moment repent her refusal,
line 7142: or feel the slightest inclination ever to see him again. In her own past
line 7143: behaviour, there was a constant source of vexation and regret; and in

line 7145: They were hopeless of remedy. Her father, contented with laughing at
line 7146: them, would never exert himself to restrain the wild giddiness of his
line 7147: youngest daughters; and her mother, with manners so far from right
line 7148: herself, was entirely insensible of the evil. Elizabeth had frequently
line 7149: united with Jane in an endeavour to check the imprudence of Catherine
line 7150: and Lydia; but while they were supported by their mother's indulgence,
line 7151: what chance could there be of improvement? Catherine, weak-spirited,
line 7152: irritable, and completely under Lydia's guidance, had been always
line 7153: affronted by their advice; and Lydia, self-willed and careless, would
line 7154: scarcely give them a hearing. They were ignorant, idle, and vain. While
line 7155: there was an officer in Meryton, they would flirt with him; and while
line 7156: Meryton was within a walk of Longbourn, they would be going there

line 7158: 
line 7159: Anxiety on Jane's behalf was another prevailing concern; and Mr. Darcy's

line 7163: could attach to the implicitness of his confidence in his friend. How
line 7164: grievous then was the thought that, of a situation so desirable in every
line 7165: respect, so replete with advantage, so promising for happiness, Jane had

line 7167: 
line 7168: When to these recollections was added the development of Wickham's

line 7170: seldom been depressed before, were now so much affected as to make it

line 7172: 
line 7173: Their engagements at Rosings were as frequent during the last week of

line 7176: their journey, gave them directions as to the best method of packing,
line 7177: and was so urgent on the necessity of placing gowns in the only right

line 7180: 
line 7181: When they parted, Lady Catherine, with great condescension, wished them
line 7182: a good journey, and invited them to come to Hunsford again next year;
