line 7185: 
line 7186: 
line 7187: 
line 7188: Chapter 38
line 7189: 
line 7190: 
line 7191: On Saturday morning Elizabeth and Mr. Collins met for breakfast a few

line 7194: 
line 7195: “I know not, Miss Elizabeth,” said he, “whether Mrs. Collins has yet

line 7197: certain you will not leave the house without receiving her thanks for
line 7198: it. The favour of your company has been much felt, I assure you. We
line 7199: know how little there is to tempt anyone to our humble abode. Our plain
line 7200: manner of living, our small rooms and few domestics, and the little we
line 7201: see of the world, must make Hunsford extremely dull to a young lady like
line 7202: yourself; but I hope you will believe us grateful for the condescension,

line 7204: your time unpleasantly.”
line 7205: 
line 7206: Elizabeth was eager with her thanks and assurances of happiness. She
line 7207: had spent six weeks with great enjoyment; and the pleasure of being with
line 7208: Charlotte, and the kind attentions she had received, must make _her_
line 7209: feel the obliged. Mr. Collins was gratified, and with a more smiling
line 7210: solemnity replied:
line 7211: 
line 7212: “It gives me great pleasure to hear that you have passed your time not
line 7213: disagreeably. We have certainly done our best; and most fortunately

line 7221: that, with all the disadvantages of this humble parsonage, I should
line 7222: not think anyone abiding in it an object of compassion, while they are
line 7223: sharers of our intimacy at Rosings.”
line 7224: 
line 7225: Words were insufficient for the elevation of his feelings; and he was

line 7228:

line 7250:

line 7265: 
line 7266: Elizabeth made no objection; the door was then allowed to be shut, and

line 7268: 
line 7269: “Good gracious!” cried Maria, after a few minutes' silence, “it seems

line 7272: 
line 7273: “A great many indeed,” said her companion with a sigh.
line 7274: 
line 7275: “We have dined nine times at Rosings, besides drinking tea there twice!
line 7276: How much I shall have to tell!”
line 7277:

line 7279: 
line 7280: Their journey was performed without much conversation, or any alarm; and
line 7281: within four hours of their leaving Hunsford they reached Mr. Gardiner's

line 7283: 
line 7284: Jane looked well, and Elizabeth had little opportunity of studying her

line 7287: Longbourn there would be leisure enough for observation.
line 7288: 
line 7289: It was not without an effort, meanwhile, that she could wait even for

line 7296: and her fear, if she once entered on the subject, of being hurried

line 7299:

line 7301: 
line 7302: Chapter 39
line 7303: 
line 7304: 
line 7305: It was the second week in May, in which the three young ladies set out
line 7306: together from Gracechurch Street for the town of ----, in Hertfordshire;
line 7307: and, as they drew near the appointed inn where Mr. Bennet's carriage
line 7308: was to meet them, they quickly perceived, in token of the coachman's
line 7309: punctuality, both Kitty and Lydia looking out of a dining-room up stairs.
line 7310: These two girls had been above an hour in the place, happily employed
line 7311: in visiting an opposite milliner, watching the sentinel on guard, and
line 7312: dressing a salad and cucumber.
line 7313: 
line 7314: After welcoming their sisters, they triumphantly displayed a table set
line 7315: out with such cold meat as an inn larder usually affords, exclaiming,
line 7316: “Is not this nice? Is not this an agreeable surprise?”
line 7317: 
line 7318: “And we mean to treat you all,” added Lydia, “but you must lend us the

line 7324: 
line 7325: And when her sisters abused it as ugly, she added, with perfect

line 7348: 
line 7349: Jane and Elizabeth looked at each other, and the waiter was told he need

line 7351:

line 7412: Their reception at home was most kind. Mrs. Bennet rejoiced to see Jane

line 7427: 
line 7428: “Oh! Mary,” said she, “I wish you had gone with us, for we had such fun!

line 7902: 
line 7903: Had Elizabeth's opinion been all drawn from her own family, she could
line 7904: not have formed a very pleasing opinion of conjugal felicity or domestic
line 7905: comfort. Her father, captivated by youth and beauty, and that appearance
line 7906: of good humour which youth and beauty generally give, had married a
line 7907: woman whose weak understanding and illiberal mind had very early in
line 7908: their marriage put an end to all real affection for her. Respect,
line 7909: esteem, and confidence had vanished for ever; and all his views
line 7910: of domestic happiness were overthrown. But Mr. Bennet was not of
line 7911: a disposition to seek comfort for the disappointment which his own
line 7912: imprudence had brought on, in any of those pleasures which too often
line 7913: console the unfortunate for their folly or their vice. He was fond of
line 7914: the country and of books; and from these tastes had arisen his principal
line 7915: enjoyments. To his wife he was very little otherwise indebted, than as
line 7916: her ignorance and folly had contributed to his amusement. This is not
line 7917: the sort of happiness which a man would in general wish to owe to his
line 7918: wife; but where other powers of entertainment are wanting, the true
line 7919: philosopher will derive benefit from such as are given.
line 7920: 
line 7921: Elizabeth, however, had never been blind to the impropriety of her
line 7922: father's behaviour as a husband. She had always seen it with pain; but
line 7923: respecting his abilities, and grateful for his affectionate treatment of
line 7924: herself, she endeavoured to forget what she could not overlook, and to
line 7925: banish from her thoughts that continual breach of conjugal obligation
line 7926: and decorum which, in exposing his wife to the contempt of her own
line 7927: children, was so highly reprehensible. But she had never felt so
line 7928: strongly as now the disadvantages which must attend the children of so
line 7929: unsuitable a marriage, nor ever been so fully aware of the evils arising
line 7930: from so ill-judged a direction of talents; talents, which, rightly used,
line 7931: might at least have preserved the respectability of his daughters, even
line 7932: if incapable of enlarging the mind of his wife.
line 7933: 
line 7934: When Elizabeth had rejoiced over Wickham's departure she found little
line 7935: other cause for satisfaction in the loss of the regiment. Their parties
line 7936: abroad were less varied than before, and at home she had a mother and
line 7937: sister whose constant repinings at the dullness of everything around
line 7938: them threw a real gloom over their domestic circle; and, though Kitty
line 7939: might in time regain her natural degree of sense, since the disturbers
line 7940: of her brain were removed, her other sister, from whose disposition
line 7941: greater evil might be apprehended, was likely to be hardened in all
line 7942: her folly and assurance by a situation of such double danger as a
line 7943: watering-place and a camp. Upon the whole, therefore, she found, what
line 7944: has been sometimes found before, that an event to which she had been
line 7945: looking with impatient desire did not, in taking place, bring all the
line 7946: satisfaction she had promised herself. It was consequently necessary to
line 7947: name some other period for the commencement of actual felicity--to have
line 7948: some other point on which her wishes and hopes might be fixed, and by
line 7949: again enjoying the pleasure of anticipation, console herself for the
line 7950: present, and prepare for another disappointment. Her tour to the Lakes
line 7951: was now the object of her happiest thoughts; it was her best consolation
line 7952: for all the uncomfortable hours which the discontentedness of her mother
line 7953: and Kitty made inevitable; and could she have included Jane in the
line 7954: scheme, every part of it would have been perfect.
line 7955: 
line 7956: “But it is fortunate,” thought she, “that I have something to wish for.
line 7957: Were the whole arrangement complete, my disappointment would be certain.
line 7958: But here, by carrying with me one ceaseless source of regret in my
line 7959: sister's absence, I may reasonably hope to have all my expectations of
line 7960: pleasure realised. A scheme of which every part promises delight can
line 7961: never be successful; and general disappointment is only warded off by
line 7962: the defence of some little peculiar vexation.”
line 7963: 
line 7964: When Lydia went away she promised to write very often and very minutely
line 7965: to her mother and Kitty; but her letters were always long expected, and
line 7966: always very short. Those to her mother contained little else than that
line 7967: they were just returned from the library, where such and such officers
line 7968: had attended them, and where she had seen such beautiful ornaments as
line 7969: made her quite wild; that she had a new gown, or a new parasol, which
line 7970: she would have described more fully, but was obliged to leave off in a
line 7971: violent hurry, as Mrs. Forster called her, and they were going off to
line 7972: the camp; and from her correspondence with her sister, there was still
line 7973: less to be learnt--for her letters to Kitty, though rather longer, were
line 7974: much too full of lines under the words to be made public.
line 7975: 
line 7976: After the first fortnight or three weeks of her absence, health, good
line 7977: humour, and cheerfulness began to reappear at Longbourn. Everything wore
line 7978: a happier aspect. The families who had been in town for the winter came
line 7979: back again, and summer finery and summer engagements arose. Mrs. Bennet
line 7980: was restored to her usual querulous serenity; and, by the middle of
line 7981: June, Kitty was so much recovered as to be able to enter Meryton without
line 7982: tears; an event of such happy promise as to make Elizabeth hope that by
line 7983: the following Christmas she might be so tolerably reasonable as not to
line 7984: mention an officer above once a day, unless, by some cruel and malicious
line 7985: arrangement at the War Office, another regiment should be quartered in
line 7986: Meryton.
line 7987: 
line 7988: The time fixed for the beginning of their northern tour was now fast
line 7989: approaching, and a fortnight only was wanting of it, when a letter
line 7990: arrived from Mrs. Gardiner, which at once delayed its commencement and
line 7991: curtailed its extent. Mr. Gardiner would be prevented by business from
line 7992: setting out till a fortnight later in July, and must be in London again
line 7993: within a month, and as that left too short a period for them to go so
line 7994: far, and see so much as they had proposed, or at least to see it with
line 7995: the leisure and comfort they had built on, they were obliged to give up
line 7996: the Lakes, and substitute a more contracted tour, and, according to the
line 7997: present plan, were to go no farther northwards than Derbyshire. In that
line 7998: county there was enough to be seen to occupy the chief of their three
line 7999: weeks; and to Mrs. Gardiner it had a peculiarly strong attraction. The
line 8000: town where she had formerly passed some years of her life, and where
line 8001: they were now to spend a few days, was probably as great an object of
line 8002: her curiosity as all the celebrated beauties of Matlock, Chatsworth,
line 8003: Dovedale, or the Peak.
line 8004: 
line 8005: Elizabeth was excessively disappointed; she had set her heart on seeing
line 8006: the Lakes, and still thought there might have been time enough. But it
line 8007: was her business to be satisfied--and certainly her temper to be happy;
line 8008: and all was soon right again.
line 8009: 
line 8010: With the mention of Derbyshire there were many ideas connected. It was
line 8011: impossible for her to see the word without thinking of Pemberley and its
line 8012: owner. “But surely,” said she, “I may enter his county with impunity,
line 8013: and rob it of a few petrified spars without his perceiving me.”
line 8014: 
line 8015: The period of expectation was now doubled. Four weeks were to pass away
line 8016: before her uncle and aunt's arrival. But they did pass away, and Mr.
line 8017: and Mrs. Gardiner, with their four children, did at length appear at
line 8018: Longbourn. The children, two girls of six and eight years old, and two
line 8019: younger boys, were to be left under the particular care of their
line 8020: cousin Jane, who was the general favourite, and whose steady sense and
line 8021: sweetness of temper exactly adapted her for attending to them in every
line 8022: way--teaching them, playing with them, and loving them.
line 8023: 
line 8024: The Gardiners stayed only one night at Longbourn, and set off the
line 8025: next morning with Elizabeth in pursuit of novelty and amusement.
line 8026: One enjoyment was certain--that of suitableness of companions;
line 8027: a suitableness which comprehended health and temper to bear
line 8028: inconveniences--cheerfulness to enhance every pleasure--and affection
line 8029: and intelligence, which might supply it among themselves if there were
line 8030: disappointments abroad.
line 8031: 
line 8032: It is not the object of this work to give a description of Derbyshire,
line 8033: nor of any of the remarkable places through which their route thither
line 8034: lay; Oxford, Blenheim, Warwick, Kenilworth, Birmingham, etc. are
line 8035: sufficiently known. A small part of Derbyshire is all the present
line 8036: concern. To the little town of Lambton, the scene of Mrs. Gardiner's
line 8037: former residence, and where she had lately learned some acquaintance
line 8038: still remained, they bent their steps, after having seen all the
line 8039: principal wonders of the country; and within five miles of Lambton,
line 8040: Elizabeth found from her aunt that Pemberley was situated. It was not
line 8041: in their direct road, nor more than a mile or two out of it. In
line 8042: talking over their route the evening before, Mrs. Gardiner expressed
line 8043: an inclination to see the place again. Mr. Gardiner declared his
line 8044: willingness, and Elizabeth was applied to for her approbation.
line 8045: 
line 8046: “My love, should not you like to see a place of which you have heard
line 8047: so much?” said her aunt; “a place, too, with which so many of your
line 8048: acquaintances are connected. Wickham passed all his youth there, you
line 8049: know.”
line 8050: 
line 8051: Elizabeth was distressed. She felt that she had no business at
line 8052: Pemberley, and was obliged to assume a disinclination for seeing it. She
line 8053: must own that she was tired of seeing great houses; after going over so
line 8054: many, she really had no pleasure in fine carpets or satin curtains.
line 8055: 
line 8056: Mrs. Gardiner abused her stupidity. “If it were merely a fine house
line 8057: richly furnished,” said she, “I should not care about it myself; but
line 8058: the grounds are delightful. They have some of the finest woods in the
line 8059: country.”
line 8060: 
line 8061: Elizabeth said no more--but her mind could not acquiesce. The
line 8062: possibility of meeting Mr. Darcy, while viewing the place, instantly
line 8063: occurred. It would be dreadful! She blushed at the very idea, and
line 8064: thought it would be better to speak openly to her aunt than to run such
line 8065: a risk. But against this there were objections; and she finally resolved
line 8066: that it could be the last resource, if her private inquiries to the
line 8067: absence of the family were unfavourably answered.
line 8068: 
line 8069: Accordingly, when she retired at night, she asked the chambermaid
line 8070: whether Pemberley were not a very fine place? what was the name of its
line 8071: proprietor? and, with no little alarm, whether the family were down for
line 8072: the summer? A most welcome negative followed the last question--and her
line 8073: alarms now being removed, she was at leisure to feel a great deal of
line 8074: curiosity to see the house herself; and when the subject was revived the
line 8075: next morning, and she was again applied to, could readily answer, and
line 8076: with a proper air of indifference, that she had not really any dislike
line 8077: to the scheme. To Pemberley, therefore, they were to go.
line 8078: 
line 8079: 
line 8080: 
line 8081: Chapter 43
line 8082: 
line 8083: 
line 8084: Elizabeth, as they drove along, watched for the first appearance of
line 8085: Pemberley Woods with some perturbation; and when at length they turned
line 8086: in at the lodge, her spirits were in a high flutter.
line 8087: 
line 8088: The park was very large, and contained great variety of ground. They
line 8089: entered it in one of its lowest points, and drove for some time through
line 8090: a beautiful wood stretching over a wide extent.
line 8091: 
line 8092: Elizabeth's mind was too full for conversation, but she saw and admired
line 8093: every remarkable spot and point of view. They gradually ascended for
line 8094: half-a-mile, and then found themselves at the top of a considerable
line 8095: eminence, where the wood ceased, and the eye was instantly caught by
line 8096: Pemberley House, situated on the opposite side of a valley, into which
line 8097: the road with some abruptness wound. It was a large, handsome stone
line 8098: building, standing well on rising ground, and backed by a ridge of
line 8099: high woody hills; and in front, a stream of some natural importance was
line 8100: swelled into greater, but without any artificial appearance. Its banks
line 8101: were neither formal nor falsely adorned. Elizabeth was delighted. She
line 8102: had never seen a place for which nature had done more, or where natural
line 8103: beauty had been so little counteracted by an awkward taste. They were
line 8104: all of them warm in their admiration; and at that moment she felt that
line 8105: to be mistress of Pemberley might be something!
line 8106: 
line 8107: They descended the hill, crossed the bridge, and drove to the door; and,
line 8108: while examining the nearer aspect of the house, all her apprehension of
line 8109: meeting its owner returned. She dreaded lest the chambermaid had been
line 8110: mistaken. On applying to see the place, they were admitted into the
line 8111: hall; and Elizabeth, as they waited for the housekeeper, had leisure to
line 8112: wonder at her being where she was.
line 8113: 
line 8114: The housekeeper came; a respectable-looking elderly woman, much less
line 8115: fine, and more civil, than she had any notion of finding her. They
line 8116: followed her into the dining-parlour. It was a large, well proportioned
line 8117: room, handsomely fitted up. Elizabeth, after slightly surveying it, went
line 8118: to a window to enjoy its prospect. The hill, crowned with wood, which
line 8119: they had descended, receiving increased abruptness from the distance,
line 8120: was a beautiful object. Every disposition of the ground was good; and
line 8121: she looked on the whole scene, the river, the trees scattered on its
line 8122: banks and the winding of the valley, as far as she could trace it,
line 8123: with delight. As they passed into other rooms these objects were taking
line 8124: different positions; but from every window there were beauties to be
line 8125: seen. The rooms were lofty and handsome, and their furniture suitable to
line 8126: the fortune of its proprietor; but Elizabeth saw, with admiration of
line 8127: his taste, that it was neither gaudy nor uselessly fine; with less of
line 8128: splendour, and more real elegance, than the furniture of Rosings.
line 8129: 
line 8130: “And of this place,” thought she, “I might have been mistress! With
line 8131: these rooms I might now have been familiarly acquainted! Instead of
line 8132: viewing them as a stranger, I might have rejoiced in them as my own, and
line 8133: welcomed to them as visitors my uncle and aunt. But no,”--recollecting
line 8134: herself--“that could never be; my uncle and aunt would have been lost to
line 8135: me; I should not have been allowed to invite them.”
line 8136: 
line 8137: This was a lucky recollection--it saved her from something very like
line 8138: regret.
line 8139: 
line 8140: She longed to inquire of the housekeeper whether her master was really
line 8141: absent, but had not the courage for it. At length however, the question
line 8142: was asked by her uncle; and she turned away with alarm, while Mrs.
line 8143: Reynolds replied that he was, adding, “But we expect him to-morrow, with
line 8144: a large party of friends.” How rejoiced was Elizabeth that their own
line 8145: journey had not by any circumstance been delayed a day!
line 8146: 
line 8147: Her aunt now called her to look at a picture. She approached and saw the
line 8148: likeness of Mr. Wickham, suspended, amongst several other miniatures,
line 8149: over the mantelpiece. Her aunt asked her, smilingly, how she liked it.
line 8150: The housekeeper came forward, and told them it was a picture of a young
line 8151: gentleman, the son of her late master's steward, who had been brought
line 8152: up by him at his own expense. “He is now gone into the army,” she added;
line 8153: “but I am afraid he has turned out very wild.”
line 8154: 
line 8155: Mrs. Gardiner looked at her niece with a smile, but Elizabeth could not
line 8156: return it.
line 8157: 
line 8158: “And that,” said Mrs. Reynolds, pointing to another of the miniatures,
line 8159: “is my master--and very like him. It was drawn at the same time as the
line 8160: other--about eight years ago.”
line 8161: 
line 8162: “I have heard much of your master's fine person,” said Mrs. Gardiner,
line 8163: looking at the picture; “it is a handsome face. But, Lizzy, you can tell
line 8164: us whether it is like or not.”
line 8165: 
line 8166: Mrs. Reynolds respect for Elizabeth seemed to increase on this
line 8167: intimation of her knowing her master.
line 8168: 
line 8169: “Does that young lady know Mr. Darcy?”
line 8170: 
line 8171: Elizabeth coloured, and said: “A little.”
line 8172: 
line 8173: “And do not you think him a very handsome gentleman, ma'am?”
line 8174: 
line 8175: “Yes, very handsome.”
line 8176: 
line 8177: “I am sure I know none so handsome; but in the gallery up stairs you
line 8178: will see a finer, larger picture of him than this. This room was my late
line 8179: master's favourite room, and these miniatures are just as they used to
line 8180: be then. He was very fond of them.”
line 8181: 
line 8182: This accounted to Elizabeth for Mr. Wickham's being among them.
line 8183: 
line 8184: Mrs. Reynolds then directed their attention to one of Miss Darcy, drawn
line 8185: when she was only eight years old.
line 8186: 
line 8187: “And is Miss Darcy as handsome as her brother?” said Mrs. Gardiner.
line 8188: 
line 8189: “Oh! yes--the handsomest young lady that ever was seen; and so
line 8190: accomplished!--She plays and sings all day long. In the next room is
line 8191: a new instrument just come down for her--a present from my master; she
line 8192: comes here to-morrow with him.”
line 8193: 
line 8194: Mr. Gardiner, whose manners were very easy and pleasant, encouraged her
line 8195: communicativeness by his questions and remarks; Mrs. Reynolds, either
line 8196: by pride or attachment, had evidently great pleasure in talking of her
line 8197: master and his sister.
line 8198: 
line 8199: “Is your master much at Pemberley in the course of the year?”
line 8200: 
line 8201: “Not so much as I could wish, sir; but I dare say he may spend half his
line 8202: time here; and Miss Darcy is always down for the summer months.”
line 8203: 
line 8204: “Except,” thought Elizabeth, “when she goes to Ramsgate.”
line 8205: 
line 8206: “If your master would marry, you might see more of him.”
line 8207: 
line 8208: “Yes, sir; but I do not know when _that_ will be. I do not know who is
line 8209: good enough for him.”
line 8210: 
line 8211: Mr. and Mrs. Gardiner smiled. Elizabeth could not help saying, “It is
line 8212: very much to his credit, I am sure, that you should think so.”
line 8213: 
line 8214: “I say no more than the truth, and everybody will say that knows him,”
line 8215:  replied the other. Elizabeth thought this was going pretty far; and she
line 8216: listened with increasing astonishment as the housekeeper added, “I have
line 8217: never known a cross word from him in my life, and I have known him ever

line 8219: 
line 8220: This was praise, of all others most extraordinary, most opposite to her
line 8221: ideas. That he was not a good-tempered man had been her firmest opinion.
line 8222: Her keenest attention was awakened; she longed to hear more, and was
line 8223: grateful to her uncle for saying:
line 8224: 
line 8225: “There are very few people of whom so much can be said. You are lucky in
line 8226: having such a master.”
line 8227: 
line 8228: “Yes, sir, I know I am. If I were to go through the world, I could
line 8229: not meet with a better. But I have always observed, that they who are
line 8230: good-natured when children, are good-natured when they grow up; and
line 8231: he was always the sweetest-tempered, most generous-hearted boy in the
line 8232: world.”
line 8233: 
line 8234: Elizabeth almost stared at her. “Can this be Mr. Darcy?” thought she.
line 8235: 
line 8236: “His father was an excellent man,” said Mrs. Gardiner.
line 8237: 
line 8238: “Yes, ma'am, that he was indeed; and his son will be just like him--just
line 8239: as affable to the poor.”
line 8240: 
line 8241: Elizabeth listened, wondered, doubted, and was impatient for more. Mrs.

line 8245: prejudice to which he attributed her excessive commendation of her

line 8248: 
line 8249: “He is the best landlord, and the best master,” said she, “that ever
line 8250: lived; not like the wild young men nowadays, who think of nothing but
line 8251: themselves. There is not one of his tenants or servants but will give
line 8252: him a good name. Some people call him proud; but I am sure I never saw
line 8253: anything of it. To my fancy, it is only because he does not rattle away

line 8255: 
line 8256: “In what an amiable light does this place him!” thought Elizabeth.
line 8257: 
line 8258: “This fine account of him,” whispered her aunt as they walked, “is not
line 8259: quite consistent with his behaviour to our poor friend.”
line 8260: 
line 8261: “Perhaps we might be deceived.”
line 8262: 
line 8263: “That is not very likely; our authority was too good.”
line 8264: 
line 8265: On reaching the spacious lobby above they were shown into a very pretty
line 8266: sitting-room, lately fitted up with greater elegance and lightness than
line 8267: the apartments below; and were informed that it was but just done to
line 8268: give pleasure to Miss Darcy, who had taken a liking to the room when
line 8269: last at Pemberley.
line 8270: 
line 8271: “He is certainly a good brother,” said Elizabeth, as she walked towards
line 8272: one of the windows.
line 8273: 
line 8274: Mrs. Reynolds anticipated Miss Darcy's delight, when she should enter
line 8275: the room. “And this is always the way with him,” she added. “Whatever
line 8276: can give his sister any pleasure is sure to be done in a moment. There
line 8277: is nothing he would not do for her.”
line 8278: 
line 8279: The picture-gallery, and two or three of the principal bedrooms, were

line 8281: but Elizabeth knew nothing of the art; and from such as had been already
line 8282: visible below, she had willingly turned to look at some drawings of Miss
line 8283: Darcy's, in crayons, whose subjects were usually more interesting, and
line 8284: also more intelligible.
line 8285: 
line 8286: In the gallery there were many family portraits, but they could have
line 8287: little to fix the attention of a stranger. Elizabeth walked in quest of
line 8288: the only face whose features would be known to her. At last it arrested
line 8289: her--and she beheld a striking resemblance to Mr. Darcy, with such a
line 8290: smile over the face as she remembered to have sometimes seen when he
line 8291: looked at her. She stood several minutes before the picture, in earnest
line 8292: contemplation, and returned to it again before they quitted the gallery.
line 8293: Mrs. Reynolds informed them that it had been taken in his father's
line 8294: lifetime.
line 8295: 
line 8296: There was certainly at this moment, in Elizabeth's mind, a more gentle
line 8297: sensation towards the original than she had ever felt at the height of
line 8298: their acquaintance. The commendation bestowed on him by Mrs. Reynolds
line 8299: was of no trifling nature. What praise is more valuable than the praise
line 8300: of an intelligent servant? As a brother, a landlord, a master, she
line 8301: considered how many people's happiness were in his guardianship!--how
line 8302: much of pleasure or pain was it in his power to bestow!--how much of
line 8303: good or evil must be done by him! Every idea that had been brought
line 8304: forward by the housekeeper was favourable to his character, and as she
line 8305: stood before the canvas on which he was represented, and fixed his
line 8306: eyes upon herself, she thought of his regard with a deeper sentiment of
line 8307: gratitude than it had ever raised before; she remembered its warmth, and
line 8308: softened its impropriety of expression.
line 8309: 
line 8310: When all of the house that was open to general inspection had been seen,
line 8311: they returned downstairs, and, taking leave of the housekeeper, were
line 8312: consigned over to the gardener, who met them at the hall-door.
line 8313: 
line 8314: As they walked across the hall towards the river, Elizabeth turned back
line 8315: to look again; her uncle and aunt stopped also, and while the former
line 8316: was conjecturing as to the date of the building, the owner of it himself
line 8317: suddenly came forward from the road, which led behind it to the stables.
line 8318: 
line 8319: They were within twenty yards of each other, and so abrupt was his
line 8320: appearance, that it was impossible to avoid his sight. Their eyes
line 8321: instantly met, and the cheeks of both were overspread with the deepest
line 8322: blush. He absolutely started, and for a moment seemed immovable from
line 8323: surprise; but shortly recovering himself, advanced towards the party,
line 8324: and spoke to Elizabeth, if not in terms of perfect composure, at least
line 8325: of perfect civility.
line 8326: 
line 8327: She had instinctively turned away; but stopping on his approach,
line 8328: received his compliments with an embarrassment impossible to be
line 8329: overcome. Had his first appearance, or his resemblance to the picture
line 8330: they had just been examining, been insufficient to assure the other two
line 8331: that they now saw Mr. Darcy, the gardener's expression of surprise, on
line 8332: beholding his master, must immediately have told it. They stood a little
line 8333: aloof while he was talking to their niece, who, astonished and confused,
line 8334: scarcely dared lift her eyes to his face, and knew not what answer
line 8335: she returned to his civil inquiries after her family. Amazed at the
line 8336: alteration of his manner since they last parted, every sentence that
line 8337: he uttered was increasing her embarrassment; and every idea of the
line 8338: impropriety of her being found there recurring to her mind, the few
line 8339: minutes in which they continued were some of the most uncomfortable in
line 8340: her life. Nor did he seem much more at ease; when he spoke, his accent
line 8341: had none of its usual sedateness; and he repeated his inquiries as
line 8342: to the time of her having left Longbourn, and of her having stayed in
line 8343: Derbyshire, so often, and in so hurried a way, as plainly spoke the
line 8344: distraction of his thoughts.
line 8345: 
line 8346: At length every idea seemed to fail him; and, after standing a few
line 8347: moments without saying a word, he suddenly recollected himself, and took
line 8348: leave.
line 8349: 
line 8350: The others then joined her, and expressed admiration of his figure; but
line 8351: Elizabeth heard not a word, and wholly engrossed by her own feelings,
line 8352: followed them in silence. She was overpowered by shame and vexation. Her
line 8353: coming there was the most unfortunate, the most ill-judged thing in the
line 8354: world! How strange it must appear to him! In what a disgraceful light
line 8355: might it not strike so vain a man! It might seem as if she had purposely
line 8356: thrown herself in his way again! Oh! why did she come? Or, why did he
line 8357: thus come a day before he was expected? Had they been only ten minutes
line 8358: sooner, they should have been beyond the reach of his discrimination;
line 8359: for it was plain that he was that moment arrived--that moment alighted
line 8360: from his horse or his carriage. She blushed again and again over
line 8361: the perverseness of the meeting. And his behaviour, so strikingly
line 8362: altered--what could it mean? That he should even speak to her was

line 8366: a contrast did it offer to his last address in Rosings Park, when he put

line 8591: Elizabeth had settled it that Mr. Darcy would bring his sister to visit

line 8594: But her conclusion was false; for on the very morning after their

line 8605: the business. Nothing had ever suggested it before, but they felt that
line 8606: there was no other way of accounting for such attentions from such a

line 8611: she dreaded lest the partiality of the brother should have said too much

line 8626:

line 8752: was a liberal man, and did much good among the poor.
line 8753: 
line 8754: With respect to Wickham, the travellers soon found that he was not held
line 8755: there in much estimation; for though the chief of his concerns with the
line 8756: son of his patron were imperfectly understood, it was yet a well-known

line 8759: 
line 8760: As for Elizabeth, her thoughts were at Pemberley this evening more than

line 8763: and she lay awake two whole hours endeavouring to make them out. She

line 8773: It was gratitude; gratitude, not merely for having once loved her,
line 8774: but for loving her still well enough to forgive all the petulance and

line 8780: were concerned, was soliciting the good opinion of her friends, and bent

line 8782: pride exciting not only astonishment but gratitude--for to love, ardent

line 8784: sort to be encouraged, as by no means unpleasing, though it could not be
line 8785: exactly defined. She respected, she esteemed, she was grateful to him,
line 8786: she felt a real interest in his welfare; and she only wanted to know how
line 8787: far she wished that welfare to depend upon herself, and how far it would
line 8788: be for the happiness of both that she should employ the power, which her
line 8789: fancy told her she still possessed, of bringing on her the renewal of

line 9226: She was wild to be at home--to hear, to see, to be upon the spot to

line 9270: 
line 9271: “I have been thinking it over again, Elizabeth,” said her uncle, as they

line 9679: “He meant I believe,” replied Jane, “to go to Epsom, the place where
line 9680: they last changed horses, see the postilions and try if anything could
line 9681: be made out from them. His principal object must be to discover the
line 9682: number of the hackney coach which took them from Clapham. It had come
line 9683: with a fare from London; and as he thought that the circumstance of a
line 9684: gentleman and lady's removing from one carriage into another might
line 9685: be remarked he meant to make inquiries at Clapham. If he could anyhow
line 9686: discover at what house the coachman had before set down his fare, he
line 9687: determined to make inquiries there, and hoped it might not be impossible
line 9688: to find out the stand and number of the coach. I do not know of any
line 9689: other designs that he had formed; but he was in such a hurry to be gone,
line 9690: and his spirits so greatly discomposed, that I had difficulty in finding

line 9693: 
line 9694: 
line 9695: Chapter 48
line 9696: 
line 9697: 
line 9698: The whole party were in hopes of a letter from Mr. Bennet the next
line 9699: morning, but the post came in without bringing a single line from him.
line 9700: His family knew him to be, on all common occasions, a most negligent and
line 9701: dilatory correspondent; but at such a time they had hoped for exertion.
line 9702: They were forced to conclude that he had no pleasing intelligence to
line 9703: send; but even of _that_ they would have been glad to be certain. Mr.
line 9704: Gardiner had waited only for the letters before he set off.
line 9705: 
line 9706: When he was gone, they were certain at least of receiving constant
line 9707: information of what was going on, and their uncle promised, at parting,

line 9710: security for her husband's not being killed in a duel.
line 9711: 
line 9712: Mrs. Gardiner and the children were to remain in Hertfordshire a few
line 9713: days longer, as the former thought her presence might be serviceable
line 9714: to her nieces. She shared in their attendance on Mrs. Bennet, and was a

line 9717: cheering and heartening them up--though, as she never came without

line 9721: 
line 9722: All Meryton seemed striving to blacken the man who, but three months

line 9726: Everybody declared that he was the wickedest young man in the world;
line 9727: and everybody began to find out that they had always distrusted the
line 9728: appearance of his goodness. Elizabeth, though she did not credit above
line 9729: half of what was said, believed enough to make her former assurance of
line 9730: her sister's ruin more certain; and even Jane, who believed still less
line 9731: of it, became almost hopeless, more especially as the time was now come
line 9732: when, if they had gone to Scotland, which she had never before entirely
line 9733: despaired of, they must in all probability have gained some news of
line 9734: them.
line 9735: 
line 9736: Mr. Gardiner left Longbourn on Sunday; on Tuesday his wife received a
line 9737: letter from him; it told them that, on his arrival, he had immediately

line 9741: determined to inquire at all the principal hotels in town, as Mr. Bennet
line 9742: thought it possible they might have gone to one of them, on their first
line 9743: coming to London, before they procured lodgings. Mr. Gardiner himself

line 9745: eager in it, he meant to assist him in pursuing it. He added that Mr.
line 9746: Bennet seemed wholly disinclined at present to leave London and promised

line 9748: 
line 9749: “I have written to Colonel Forster to desire him to find out, if
line 9750: possible, from some of the young man's intimates in the regiment,

line 9768: 
line 9769: Every day at Longbourn was now a day of anxiety; but the most anxious

line 9772: whatever of good or bad was to be told would be communicated, and every

line 9774:

line 9782: “MY DEAR SIR,
line 9783:

line 9816: Mr. Gardiner did not write again till he had received an answer from

line 9833: 
line 9834: Mr. Gardiner added in his letter, that they might expect to see their

line 9842:

line 9846: 
line 9847: As Mrs. Gardiner began to wish to be at home, it was settled that she

line 9866: 
line 9867: When Mr. Bennet arrived, he had all the appearance of his usual

line 9872:

line 9879: “You must not be too severe upon yourself,” replied Elizabeth.

line 9889:

line 9891: 
line 9892: “She is happy then,” said her father drily; “and her residence there

line 9894: 
line 9895: Then after a short silence he continued:
line 9896:

line 9902:

line 9928: 
line 9929:

line 10148: “Well,” cried her mother, “it is all very right; who should do it but

line 10164:

line 10173: Mrs. Hill began instantly to express her joy. Elizabeth received her

line 10176: 
line 10177: Poor Lydia's situation must, at best, be bad enough; but that it was

line 10188: 
line 10189: Mr. Bennet had very often wished before this period of his life that,
line 10190: instead of spending his whole income, he had laid by an annual sum for
line 10191: the better provision of his children, and of his wife, if she survived
line 10192: him. He now wished it more than ever. Had he done his duty in that
line 10193: respect, Lydia need not have been indebted to her uncle for whatever
line 10194: of honour or credit could now be purchased for her. The satisfaction of
line 10195: prevailing on one of the most worthless young men in Great Britain to be
line 10196: her husband might then have rested in its proper place.
line 10197: 
line 10198: He was seriously concerned that a cause of so little advantage to anyone
line 10199: should be forwarded at the sole expense of his brother-in-law, and he
line 10200: was determined, if possible, to find out the extent of his assistance,
line 10201: and to discharge the obligation as soon as he could.
line 10202: 
line 10203: When first Mr. Bennet had married, economy was held to be perfectly
line 10204: useless, for, of course, they were to have a son. The son was to join
line 10205: in cutting off the entail, as soon as he should be of age, and the widow
line 10206: and younger children would by that means be provided for. Five daughters
line 10207: successively entered the world, but yet the son was to come; and Mrs.
line 10208: Bennet, for many years after Lydia's birth, had been certain that he
line 10209: would. This event had at last been despaired of, but it was then
line 10210: too late to be saving. Mrs. Bennet had no turn for economy, and her
line 10211: husband's love of independence had alone prevented their exceeding their
line 10212: income.
line 10213: 
line 10214: Five thousand pounds was settled by marriage articles on Mrs. Bennet and
line 10215: the children. But in what proportions it should be divided amongst the
line 10216: latter depended on the will of the parents. This was one point, with
line 10217: regard to Lydia, at least, which was now to be settled, and Mr. Bennet
line 10218: could have no hesitation in acceding to the proposal before him. In

line 10220: though expressed most concisely, he then delivered on paper his perfect
line 10221: approbation of all that was done, and his willingness to fulfil the
line 10222: engagements that had been made for him. He had never before supposed
line 10223: that, could Wickham be prevailed on to marry his daughter, it would
line 10224: be done with so little inconvenience to himself as by the present
line 10225: arrangement. He would scarcely be ten pounds a year the loser by the
line 10226: hundred that was to be paid them; for, what with her board and pocket
line 10227: allowance, and the continual presents in money which passed to her
line 10228: through her mother's hands, Lydia's expenses had been very little within
line 10229: that sum.
line 10230: 
line 10231: That it would be done with such trifling exertion on his side, too, was

line 10236: dispatched; for, though dilatory in undertaking business, he was quick
line 10237: in its execution. He begged to know further particulars of what he
line 10238: was indebted to his brother, but was too angry with Lydia to send any
line 10239: message to her.
line 10240: 
line 10241: The good news spread quickly through the house, and with proportionate

line 10245: happiest alternative, been secluded from the world, in some distant

line 10251: 
line 10252: It was a fortnight since Mrs. Bennet had been downstairs; but on this

line 10263: 
line 10264: “Haye Park might do,” said she, “if the Gouldings could quit it--or the

line 10268: 
line 10269: Her husband allowed her to talk on without interruption while the

line 10288: 
line 10289: Elizabeth was now most heartily sorry that she had, from the distress of

line 10295: 
line 10296: She had no fear of its spreading farther through his means. There were

line 10306: 
line 10307: From such a connection she could not wonder that he would shrink. The

line 10316: 
line 10317: What a triumph for him, as she often thought, could he know that the

line 10322:

line 10330: 
line 10331: But no such happy marriage could now teach the admiring multitude what

line 10335: 
line 10336: How Wickham and Lydia were to be supported in tolerable independence,
line 10337: she could not imagine. But how little of permanent happiness could

line 10343: Mr. Gardiner soon wrote again to his brother. To Mr. Bennet's

line 10349:

line 10411: Their sister's wedding day arrived; and Jane and Elizabeth felt for her

line 10418:

line 10423:

line 10440:

line 11533: 
line 11534: Bingley was punctual to his appointment; and he and Mr. Bennet spent

line 11545:

line 11720: 
line 11721: One morning, about a week after Bingley's engagement with Jane had been

line 11734:

line 12941: Happy for all her maternal feelings was the day on which Mrs. Bennet got
line 12942: rid of her two most deserving daughters. With what delighted pride
line 12943: she afterwards visited Mrs. Bingley, and talked of Mrs. Darcy, may
line 12944: be guessed. I wish I could say, for the sake of her family, that the
line 12945: accomplishment of her earnest desire in the establishment of so many
line 12946: of her children produced so happy an effect as to make her a sensible,
line 12947: amiable, well-informed woman for the rest of her life; though perhaps it
line 12948: was lucky for her husband, who might not have relished domestic felicity
line 12949: in so unusual a form, that she still was occasionally nervous and
line 12950: invariably silly.
line 12951: 
line 12952: Mr. Bennet missed his second daughter exceedingly; his affection for her
line 12953: drew him oftener from home than anything else could do. He delighted in
line 12954: going to Pemberley, especially when he was least expected.
line 12955: 
line 12956: Mr. Bingley and Jane remained at Netherfield only a twelvemonth. So near
line 12957: a vicinity to her mother and Meryton relations was not desirable even to
line 12958: _his_ easy temper, or _her_ affectionate heart. The darling wish of his
line 12959: sisters was then gratified; he bought an estate in a neighbouring county
line 12960: to Derbyshire, and Jane and Elizabeth, in addition to every other source
line 12961: of happiness, were within thirty miles of each other.
line 12962: 
line 12963: Kitty, to her very material advantage, spent the chief of her time with
line 12964: her two elder sisters. In society so superior to what she had generally
line 12965: known, her improvement was great. She was not of so ungovernable a
line 12966: temper as Lydia; and, removed from the influence of Lydia's example,
line 12967: she became, by proper attention and management, less irritable, less
line 12968: ignorant, and less insipid. From the further disadvantage of Lydia's
line 12969: society she was of course carefully kept, and though Mrs. Wickham
line 12970: frequently invited her to come and stay with her, with the promise of
line 12971: balls and young men, her father would never consent to her going.
line 12972: 
line 12973: Mary was the only daughter who remained at home; and she was necessarily
line 12974: drawn from the pursuit of accomplishments by Mrs. Bennet's being quite
line 12975: unable to sit alone. Mary was obliged to mix more with the world, but
line 12976: she could still moralize over every morning visit; and as she was no
line 12977: longer mortified by comparisons between her sisters' beauty and her own,
line 12978: it was suspected by her father that she submitted to the change without
line 12979: much reluctance.
line 12980: 
line 12981: As for Wickham and Lydia, their characters suffered no revolution from
line 12982: the marriage of her sisters. He bore with philosophy the conviction that
line 12983: Elizabeth must now become acquainted with whatever of his ingratitude
line 12984: and falsehood had before been unknown to her; and in spite of every
line 12985: thing, was not wholly without hope that Darcy might yet be prevailed on
line 12986: to make his fortune. The congratulatory letter which Elizabeth received
line 12987: from Lydia on her marriage, explained to her that, by his wife at least,
line 12988: if not by himself, such a hope was cherished. The letter was to this
line 12989: effect:
line 12990: 
line 12991: “MY DEAR LIZZY,
line 12992: 
line 12993: “I wish you joy. If you love Mr. Darcy half as well as I do my dear
line 12994: Wickham, you must be very happy. It is a great comfort to have you so
line 12995: rich, and when you have nothing else to do, I hope you will think of us.
line 12996: I am sure Wickham would like a place at court very much, and I do not
line 12997: think we shall have quite money enough to live upon without some help.
line 12998: Any place would do, of about three or four hundred a year; but however,
line 12999: do not speak to Mr. Darcy about it, if you had rather not.
line 13000: 
line 13001: “Yours, etc.”
line 13002: 
line 13003: As it happened that Elizabeth had _much_ rather not, she endeavoured in
line 13004: her answer to put an end to every entreaty and expectation of the kind.
line 13005: Such relief, however, as it was in her power to afford, by the practice
line 13006: of what might be called economy in her own private expences, she
line 13007: frequently sent them. It had always been evident to her that such an
line 13008: income as theirs, under the direction of two persons so extravagant in
line 13009: their wants, and heedless of the future, must be very insufficient to
line 13010: their support; and whenever they changed their quarters, either Jane or
line 13011: herself were sure of being applied to for some little assistance
line 13012: towards discharging their bills. Their manner of living, even when the
line 13013: restoration of peace dismissed them to a home, was unsettled in the
line 13014: extreme. They were always moving from place to place in quest of a cheap
line 13015: situation, and always spending more than they ought. His affection for
line 13016: her soon sunk into indifference; hers lasted a little longer; and
line 13017: in spite of her youth and her manners, she retained all the claims to
line 13018: reputation which her marriage had given her.
line 13019: 
line 13020: Though Darcy could never receive _him_ at Pemberley, yet, for
line 13021: Elizabeth's sake, he assisted him further in his profession. Lydia was
line 13022: occasionally a visitor there, when her husband was gone to enjoy himself
line 13023: in London or Bath; and with the Bingleys they both of them frequently
line 13024: staid so long, that even Bingley's good humour was overcome, and he
line 13025: proceeded so far as to talk of giving them a hint to be gone.
line 13026: 
line 13027: Miss Bingley was very deeply mortified by Darcy's marriage; but as she
line 13028: thought it advisable to retain the right of visiting at Pemberley, she
line 13029: dropt all her resentment; was fonder than ever of Georgiana, almost as
line 13030: attentive to Darcy as heretofore, and paid off every arrear of civility
line 13031: to Elizabeth.
line 13032: 
line 13033: Pemberley was now Georgiana's home; and the attachment of the sisters
line 13034: was exactly what Darcy had hoped to see. They were able to love each
line 13035: other even as well as they intended. Georgiana had the highest opinion
line 13036: in the world of Elizabeth; though at first she often listened with
line 13037: an astonishment bordering on alarm at her lively, sportive, manner of
line 13038: talking to her brother. He, who had always inspired in herself a respect
line 13039: which almost overcame her affection, she now saw the object of open
line 13040: pleasantry. Her mind received knowledge which had never before fallen
line 13041: in her way. By Elizabeth's instructions, she began to comprehend that
line 13042: a woman may take liberties with her husband which a brother will not
line 13043: always allow in a sister more than ten years younger than himself.
line 13044: 
line 13045: Lady Catherine was extremely indignant on the marriage of her nephew;
line 13046: and as she gave way to all the genuine frankness of her character in
line 13047: her reply to the letter which announced its arrangement, she sent him
line 13048: language so very abusive, especially of Elizabeth, that for some time
line 13049: all intercourse was at an end. But at length, by Elizabeth's persuasion,
line 13050: he was prevailed on to overlook the offence, and seek a reconciliation;
line 13051: and, after a little further resistance on the part of his aunt, her
line 13052: resentment gave way, either to her affection for him, or her curiosity
line 13053: to see how his wife conducted herself; and she condescended to wait
line 13054: on them at Pemberley, in spite of that pollution which its woods had
line 13055: received, not merely from the presence of such a mistress, but the
line 13056: visits of her uncle and aunt from the city.
line 13057: 
line 13058: With the Gardiners, they were always on the most intimate terms.
line 13059: Darcy, as well as Elizabeth, really loved them; and they were both ever
line 13060: sensible of the warmest gratitude towards the persons who, by bringing
line 13061: her into Derbyshire, had been the means of uniting them.
line 13062: 
line 13063: 
line 13064: 
line 13065:
